==PAGE:1==
Character Classes and Bounded Gaps Pattern Matching
Master Thesis, Computer Science and Engineering, Technical University of Denmark
Author:
Benjamin Bogø (s184233)
s184233@student.dtu.dk
Main Supervisor:
Philip Bille
phbi@dtu.dk
Supervisor:
Inge Li Gørtz
inge@dtu.dk
2023-01-23 – 2023-07-14
Abstract
The Character classes and Bounded Gaps Pattern Matching Problem is considered. Given a text
T = T0, . . . , Tn−1 and a pattern P = P0, . . . , Pm−1, where P consists of character classes and bounded
gaps (arbitrary strings with length constraints), the problem is to find all end positions of substrings
in T that matches P. The main algorithm considered in this thesis is the algorithm by Navarro and
Raffinot from 2003. It uses O(L|Σ|⌈L/w⌉) time for preprocessing and O(n⌈L/w⌉) time for matching
P with T, where L is the maximum length of P, Σ is the alphabet and w is the size of a computer
word. The factor ⌈L/w⌉can be removed if L ≤w to obtain the currently best known bound for
matching. This is used for matching protein sequences with patterns from the PROSITE database.
In this thesis, the preprocessing is extended to also handle repeated (negated) character classes,
that are used in the PROSITE database, and its time complexity is improved to only use O(|P|+|Σ|)
time where |P| is the pattern size (number characters used to describe the pattern). Furthermore,
some unhandled edge cases that were found in the original algorithm may lead to incorrect results.
These are addressed and fixed without affecting the complexities.
Modern processors support Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) that allows to manipulate
vectors of integers. A SIMD model for operating on vectors of X elements of w bits each is presented.
The model is used to show how vectors can be used as (X ∗w)-bit integers to allow patterns with
L ≤X ∗w to be preprocessed in O(|P|⌈L/w⌉+ |Σ|) time and O(n) time for matching. Here a very
fast specialized (X ∗w)-bit subtraction that utilizes properties of the operands is presented. It is also
shown how multiple vectors can be preprocessed in O(|P|⌈L/w⌉+ |Σ|⌈L/(Xw)⌉) time and improve
the matching with a factor of X to match in O(n⌈L/(Xw)⌉) time. The SIMD model is also used
to match the pattern with the text using X parallel runs. It adds O(X) to the preprocessing and
replaces n with n + XL for the matching phase, but is much faster in practice.
Several versions were implemented, tested and analyzed in terms of their latency chain, that is
the latency of the dependency chain with the highest latency, for a specific CPU in Advanced Vector
Extension (AVX). The test results showed that patterns with L ≤8w can be processed just using
87% more time using AVX-512 compared to patterns with L ≤w. For patterns with L ≤w, AVX-512
can parallelize the simulation to obtain a 3 times speed up and up to a 6 times speed up for protein
sequences with additional tricks. An AVX-512 parallel version that allows any pattern length is twice
as fast as the original algorithm for L ≤w and it is also faster than any other non-parallel version.

1
Introduction
This thesis will focus on optimizing an existing algorithm to the Character classes and Bounded Gaps
Pattern Matching Problem. The remaining of this section describes the problem definition and related
work. Section 2 describes the notation and variables that are used through the thesis. It also describes
how to transform an ECBG pattern into a CBG pattern, a naive algorithm to solve the problem and the
main algorithm through the thesis by Navarro and Raffinot [5]. Section 3 shows how the main algorithm
can be extended to support more types of patterns while section 4 shows how the preprocessing time
can be improved. Section 5 looks at unhandled edges in the original algorithm. The main algorithm in
its base form only supports patterns up to the size of a machine word, so section 6 describes how longer
patterns can be supported using multiple words.
The main part of the thesis describes how to utilize Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) to
manipulate vectors of integers. Section 7 describes the SIMD model that will be used. Section 8 shows
how one vector of integers can be used to represent one large integer to support even longer patterns.
Section 9 then builds on top to show how any pattern length can be supported using multiple vectors.
SIMD can also be used to parallelize the algorithm which section 10 focuses on. Several versions of the
main algorithm were implemented. Section 11 shows an overview while section 12, section 13, section 14
and section 15 describes and analyzes the different versions that are implemented in the x86-64, Advanced
Vector Extension 2 (AVX2) and AVX-512 instruction sets [13]. Some versions were further optimized as
described in section 16. All versions were performance tested and the experimental results are discussed
in section 17. Finally, the thesis is wrapped up with future work in section 18 and the conclusion in
section 19.
1.1
Problem Definition
Given integers a and b such that 0 ≤a ≤b, a bounded gap x(a, b), is an arbitrary string S over the
alphabet Σ such that a ≤|S| ≤b. A character class [C], where C ⊆Σ is a non-empty set of characters,
is a string S of length 1 (|S| = 1, a single character) and the character is in the character class (S0 ∈C).
A Character classes and Bounded Gaps Pattern (CBG pattern) P is a sequence of character classes and
bounded gaps.
A CBG pattern P matches a substring S of T iff replacing all character classes [C] in P with one
character from C and replacing all bounded gaps x(a, b) in P with any string with length between a and b
(both inclusive) results in S. Given a CBG pattern P and a string T, the Character classes and Bounded
Gaps Pattern Matching Problem (CBG problem) is to find all ending positions of substrings in T that
match P.
Character classes and bounded gaps pattern are frequently used in computational biology applications.
An example of such an application is to use the CBG patterns in the PROSITE database to search for
proteins specified by those patterns. [28]
We also define an extended version of CBG patterns that better matches the patterns in the PROSITE
database. A negated character class {C}, where C ⊂Σ is a non-empty set of characters, is a string S
of length 1 with S0 /∈C. A repeated character class, denoted [C](n) where C ⊆Σ is a non-empty set of
characters and n ≥1 is an integer, is a string S where |S| = n and Si ∈C for 0 ≤i < n. Note that
the characters in S might be different characters from C. A repeated negated character class, denoted
{C}(n) where C ⊂Σ is a non-empty set of characters and n ≥1 is an integer, is a string S where |S| = n
and Si /∈C for 0 ≤i < n. An Extended Character classes and Bounded Gaps Pattern (ECBG pattern)
P is a sequence of repeated (negated) character classes and bounded gaps. This gives rise to the ECBG
problem that can be expressed very similar to the CBG problem.
4
==PAGE:6==
1.2
Related Work
The most straight forward approach to solve the CBG problem is to transform the CBG pattern P =
P0, . . . , Pm−1 into a regular expression R and then use R for matching with the string T = T0, . . . , Tn−1.
The length of the resulting regular expression R is O(|P| + L) where L is the maximum length of the
pattern, |P| = Pm−1
j=0 |Pj| and |Pj| is the size of a bounded gap or character class. The size of a bounded
gap x(aj, bj) is 1 + 1 = 2 while its maximum length is bj. The size of a character class [Cj] is |Cj| and
its maximum length is 1. Each character class [Cj] is turned into the union (|) of all characters in the
character class which has a length of O(|Cj|) = O(|Pj|) and thus O(|P|) for all character classes. Each
bounded gap x(aj, bj) is turned into a sequence of aj required characters followed by bj −aj optional
characters which has a length of O(bj) and thus O(L) for all bounded gaps. All m pattern elements
(P0, . . . , Pm−1) are concatenated which adds O(m) = O(L) in length. Then using the regular expression
matching algorithm from Thompson’s textbook [1], one obtains an algorithm for the CBG problem using
O(n(|P| + L)) time.
Navarro and Raffinot [5] gave two bit-parallel automaton-based algorithms. The first uses O(L|Σ| +
n⌈L/w⌉) time and the second uses O(L|Σ| + nL⌈L/w⌉) where L is the maximum length of the pattern,
|Σ| is the size of the alphabet and w is the length of a machine word. In case L ≤w, then ⌈L/w⌉can
be removed from the complexities, such that the first algorithm achieves O(L|Σ| + n), which is the best
known complexity for the CBG problem to my knowledge. The first is faster in theory, but the second
has the ability to skip characters which might make it faster in practice as claimed in [5].
Bille et al. [10] gave an algorithm using O(n log k + m + α) time where k is the number of character
sequences separated by bounded gaps and α is the total number (sum) of occurrences of each of the
character class sequences within T. In addition, it uses O(m + A) space where A is the sum of the lower
bounds (aj) from the bounded gaps (x(aj, bj)). Note that this algorithm handles sequences of strings –
not sequences of character classes – and thus it cannot be used directly for the CBG problem.
Haapasalo et al. [9] presented an algorithm that is able to match multiple patterns with a text. The
patterns may contain character sequences separated by variable-length gaps like the algorithm by Bille
et al. [10] but the variable-length gaps are also allowed to have ∞as the upper bound. It is a bit unclear
what the time complexity is, but it seems to be (less than) O(P
P ∈D |P| + kn) where P ∈D is a pattern
in the set of patterns D, n is the length of the text to match with and k is the number of maximal
substrings in the patterns.
Bille and Thorup [8] gave an algorithm to match a regular expression with a text.
The regular
expression is allowed to contain characters, concatenation, union, kleene star (0 or more repetitions) and
a character class repeated between x and y times. The last construct allows to represent bounded gaps
and repeated character classes. The algorithm uses O(m log k) time for preprocessing where m is the is
the size of the pattern (number of characters) and m ≤k is the number of strings (concatenation of single
characters). It then uses O(k(log w)/w + log k) time per character (times n for all characters) to check if
the regular expression matches the text. The algorithm only checks for a match and does not report end
positions, but it can likely be adapted to do so.
Thorup [6] presented a model for multimedia processors, processors supporting Single Instruction,
Multiple Data (SIMD), long before processors supported SIMD. Here, he presented how different oper-
ations could be implemented from a minimal set of operations. Most notably for this thesis, Thorup
showed how one can perform X ∗w-bit integer addition when viewing X w-bit registers as X ∗w-bit
integers in constant time.
5
==PAGE:7==
2
Baseline
This section presents the baseline needed to understand the rest of the thesis. This includes the notation,
variables and base algorithms that are used.
2.1
Notation
The standard C-notation will be used for the arithmetic (+, −, ∗, / and %) and bitwise (∼, &, |, ⊕,
<< and >>) operators except the symbol for bitwise-xor is ⊕for typesetting reasons. All integers are
treated as unsigned integers unless something else is explicitly stated. This means that the shift operators
perform logical shifts (zero extension) and all comparison operators compares non-negative integers. All
array-like structures (strings, arrays, vectors, bitmask, etc.) are all zero-indexed. Exponentiation is used
to describe repetition of bits, i.e. (1403)2 = 111100011110002. Integers are specified in base 10 except
those specified as a bit repetition pattern which are base 2. Besides this, the following conventions will
be used for variable names:
 Integer variables will start with a lowercase letter, for instance i, j, k and packedCarries.
 Bitmasks (or bit strings) are represented by a word (integer) or an array of words. Bitmask variables
will start with an uppercase letter, for instance I, F and D. These might be prefixed with lowercase
n (for negated) or c (for current), for instance nF and cI. The i’th bit is the bit at position i. In
case of multiple words bitmasks (array), the i’th word is the entry at index i and is accessed as V [i]
where V is a multiple words bitmask. The j’th bit in V [i] is denoted as the bit at offset j. Hence,
position refers to the global bit position while offset refers to the local bit position within a word
with a given index.
 Vectors of integers variable names start with an uppercase letter, for instance GT, Carry and
Carries. The i’th vector element is accessed as Vi where V is a vector of integers. Note that a
vector representing the bitmask I also has the name I and might as well have the prefix c.
 Variables for arrays of vectors will also start with an uppercase letter. The i’th vector in A is
accesses as A[[i]]. The array of vectors might as well be viewed as an array of words where the i’th
word is accessed as A[i].
2.2
Variables
During this thesis, a lot of different variables will be introduced.
Below is an overview of the most
important ones, their meaning and when they are introduced. This might not make so much sense at
this point but is meant as a place to quickly look up the purpose of variables.
 (section 1.1) P = P0, . . . , Pm−1 represents an ECBG pattern of length m.
 (section 1.1) T = T0, . . . , Tn−1 is a text of length n to find matches in.
 (section 1.1) Σ is the alphabet (set of letters).
 (section 1.1) x(aj, bj) is a bounded gap (j’th element in P) matching any string with length between
aj and bj (both inclusive). In case aj = bj, then the bounded gap may be given as x(aj).
 (section 1.1) [Cj](nj) is a repeated character class (j’th element in P) matching a string of nj letters
where all letters are in the set Cj. In case nj = 1, (nj) may be omitted to represent the character
class [Cj]. In case |Cj| = 1, then the square brackets may be omitted as well.
6
==PAGE:8==
 (section 1.1) {Cj}(nj) is a repeated negated character class (j’th element in P) matching a string of
nj letters where all letters are in the set Σ \ Cj. In case nj = 1, (nj) may be omitted to represent
the negated character class {Cj}.
 (section 2.7) I* (initial gap states) is a bitmask that contains 1-bits at the positions that represent
the state where a bounded gap starts.
 (section 2.7) F* (final gap states) is a bitmask that contains 1-bits at the positions that represent
the state right after a bounded gap ends.
 (section 2.7) B is a table of bitmasks with one entry per c ∈Σ. The entry B[c]* has 1-bits at
positions that represent states with an ingoing edge accepting c.
 (section 2.7) nF is the bitwise-not of F.
 (section 2.7) M* is a bitmask with a single 1-bit representing the final state.
 (section 2.7) D* is a bitmask with where 1-bits represents active states.
 (section 4) G* (gap bits) is a bitmask with 1-bits at positions that represent states with an ingoing
edge accepting Σ – those generated by a bounded gap.
 (section 4) N* (negated positions) is a bitmask with 1-bits at positions that represent states with
an ingoing edge generated by a repeated negated character class. (N is used to flip all bits in B
that are set in N to negated those positions.)
 (section 5.3) start is the minimum number of letters before the first repeated (negated) character
class in P can be matched (in case a pattern starts with a bounded gap).
 (section 5.3) cI** is the current initial gap states i.e. D & I (to keep things cleaner).
 (section 5.3) cF** is the current final gap states i.e. F & ∼cI (to keep things cleaner).
 (section 8.2.2) GT is a vector where the i’th element is 1w if cIi > cFi and otherwise 0.
 (section 8.2.2) EQ is a vector where the i’th element is 1w if cFi = cIi and otherwise 0.
 (section 8.2.4) S (shuffle indexes) is a vector where the i’th element contains the index from where
the i’th element in cF b−cI gets its carry. Used to move carries to the correct elements.
 (section 8.2.4) U*** (underflow bits) is a vector where the i’th element contains a 1-bit at the same
offset as Ii if Ii > Fi and otherwise 0w. Used to determine if cFi−cIi underflows and thus generates
a carry.
 (section 8.2.4) O*** (offset of underflow bits) is a vector where the i’th element is the offset of the
1-bit in U and w if no 1-bit is set. Same usage as U, but results in 0w−11 as carry rather than 1w.
 (section 9.2.4) S′ (local shuffle indexes) is an array of vectors with local indexes from where to get
the carry. In case the global index would be in a vector before the current, then the local index is
set to the last element (X −1) where they carry is blended in.
* A bitmask either as a single word, an array of words, a vector of integers or an array of vectors.
** A bitmask either as a single word or a vector of integers.
*** A vector of integers or an array of vectors.
