2.4
ECBG Pattern Transformation
It is possible to transform an ECBG pattern to a CBG pattern. The transformation should be very
intuitive: Starting with a ECBG pattern P, replace all repeated character classes [Cj](nj) with nj
repetitions of [Cj] and replace all repeated negated character classes {Cj}(nj) with nj repetitions of
[Σ \ Cj] to obtain the CBG pattern P ′. It should be clear that the transformation is correct.
The transformation increases the size of the resulting pattern P ′. A bounded gap x(aj, bj) is constant
O(1) in size and is not modified so it still has a size of O(1). A repeated character class [Cj](nj) has a size
of O(|Cj|). It is transformed into nj copies of Cj and thus increases to a size of O(nj|Cj|). A repeated
negated character class {Cj}(nj) has a size of O(|Cj|). It is transformed into nj copies of (Σ \ Cj) ⊂Σ.
Hence, it now has a size of O(nj|Σ|). The largest increase in size comes from repeated negated character
classes. Therefore, the worst case increase come from patterns only containing repeated negated character
classes with one character. For instance, the ECBG pattern {a}(L) has a size of O(1) and is transformed
into a CBG pattern of size O(L|Σ|). Hence, the size increases at most with a factor of O(L|Σ|). Thus, if
ECBG patterns can be handle equally efficient as CBG patterns then there could be a large improvement.
2.5
Naive Algorithm
Before presenting the main algorithm that will be used in this paper, a naive algorithm to handle ECBG
patterns will be presented to be able to compare it with the main algorithm. The naive algorithm searches
for matches in the following way:
For each position k (in forward direction) in the text T, try to match the pattern P backwards starting
from position i ←k with j ←m −1. When a repeated character class [Cj](nj) should be matched, it
checks if the characters Ti−nj+1, . . . , Ti all are in Cj by iteration through the characters in Cj (once per
8
==PAGE:10==
character from T). If the check passes, set i ←i −nj and j ←j −1. Repeated negated character classes
{Cj}(nj) are handled in the same way except it is checked that the characters are not in Cj. When a
bounded gap x(aj, bj) should be matched, all possible gap lengths aj ≤g ≤bj are tried by skipping the
selected length of characters (backwards) i.e. setting i ←i −g and j ←j −1. In case a pattern element
cannot be matched, either by failing to match with a repeated (negated) character class or getting out of
bounds (getting before the first character T0), then stop the current attempt. Note that bounded gaps
spawns an attempt per tried gap length. If j = −1 and i ≥0, then report a match at position k, stop all
attempts for position k and continue to the next position.
Figure 2.1 shows how the algorithm matches P = [ab](2) −x(2, 5) −{ac} over Σ = {a, b, c} with
T = aacbccb for k = 3 and k = 6. For k = 3, it successfully matches {ac} with T3 = b. It then tries to
skip 2 characters, but it then gets out of bounds when trying to match with [ab](2). The same happens
when trying to skip 3 characters. Skipping more characters will also get out of bounds and thus no match
is found for k = 3. For k = 6, it successfully matches {ac} with T6 = b. It then tries skipping 2 and 3
characters, but in both cases it cannot match [ab](2). However, skipping 4 characters allows to match
with [ab](2) and a match at k = 6 is reported. Hence, there is no need to try a gap of length 5.
The algorithm uses no time for preprocessing. Checking for a match at a single position in T, an
implicit search tree is generated like the ones in Figure 2.1. A repeated character class [Cj](nj) generates
one new node that uses O(nj|Cj|) time since it has to compare each of the nj characters with all the
characters in Cj. A repeated negated character class {Cj}(nj) does the same. A bounded gap x(aj, bj)
Figure 2.1: The (implicit) search trees generated by the naive algorithm for k = 3 and k = 6 when
P = [ab](2) −x(2, 5) −{ac}, Σ = {a, b, c} and T = aacbccb.
9
==PAGE:11==
generates bj −aj +1 new nodes that each take constant O(1) time because it just have to skip characters.
An upper bound for the time needed per node is then O(L|Σ|) because the bound is tight for the pattern
P = [Σ](L).
An upper bound for the number of generated nodes is O(2L) in case the pattern is L
repetitions of x(0, 1). Both these upper bounds cannot appear at the same time, but it is possible to
start a pattern with y < L repetitions of x(0, 1) to generate as many nodes as possible and then have
[Σ](L −y) at the end to generate time consuming nodes. This gives O(2y) nodes that each uses up
to O((L −y)|Σ|) time and thus uses O(2y(L −y)|Σ|) time in total. The worst case time occurs when
y = L −1 which gives O(2L|Σ|) time. Hence, this is exponential time in the pattern max length L in
the worst case! However, the presented pattern could easily be replaced with x(0, L −1) to get a far
better bound. On the other hand, you just need [Cj] or {Cj} between each bounded gap x(0, 1) to have
a meaningful pattern. It halves the space for gaps and thus the time is improved to O(2L/2|Σ|) in the
worst case. Hence, for the whole text T, it takes O(2L/2n|Σ|) time. However, the average time usage
should be far better since whole subtrees can be pruned as soon a mismatch is found and the pattern
was constructed to give the worst possible time complexity.
2.6
Simple Bit Tricks
This section will briefly present some bit tricks to manipulate bits in a bitmask that will be used later.
Set i’th Bit in Bitmask
setBit : bitmask × int →bitmask
setBit(V, i) := V | (1 << i)
Straight forward in 2 operations, O(1) time. The type bitmask is used for an integer representing a
bitmask to keep them distinct from normal integers with type int.
Clear i’th Bit in Bitmask
clearBit : bitmask × int →bitmask
clearBit(V, i) := V & ∼(1 << i)
Straight forward in 3 operations, O(1) time.
Toggle i’th Bit in Bitmask
toggleBit : bitmask × int →bitmask
toggleBit(V, i) := V ⊕(1 << i)
In case you know a bit is set, clearing a bit can be achieved in 2 operations, O(1) time, by toggling the
bit with bitwise-xor.
Generate Bitmask with the i Lowest Bits Set
setLSBs : int →bitmask
setLSBs(i) := (1 << i) −1
setLSBs(i) := 1w >> (w −i)
In the first (left) option, the left shift generates a bitmask with the i’th bit set. Subtracting one from
that flips all bits up to the i’th bit (inclusive) and efficiently sets the first i bits in 2 operations, O(1)
10
==PAGE:12==
time. Alternatively, one can start with all bits set (1w) and then shift out the unwanted bits, also in
constant time with 2 operations. Note that bit shifts might have undefined behavior when shifting by w
or more bits. Thus, the first option might fail if i < 0 or i ≥w and the other option when i = 0 or i > w.
For the upcoming algorithms, the second approach will be used since w bits may be set but never 0 bits.
Set i’th to (i + k −1)’th Bit
setBits : bitmask × int × int →bitmask
setBits(V, i, k) := V | (setLSBs(k) << i)
Utilizing the previous function, setting any range of bits can be achieved in 2 + 2 = 4 operations, O(1)
time. For convenience, the function takes the first bit to set (i) and the number of bits to set (k) rather
than the first and last bits to set.
Extract Most Significant Bit (MSB)
msb : bitmask →int
msb(V ) := V >> (w −1)
Extracting the most significant bit (MSB), also called the sign bit when working with a bitmask as a
signed integer, is simply shifting the bit w −1 bits to the right so it is the only original bit left. This
can be achieved in 1 operation, O(1) time, assuming w −1 is precomputed. This function will mainly be
used as a way to compactly express the MSB.
2.7
Main Algorithm
The main focus of this thesis is to improve the forward algorithm presented by Gonzalo Navarro and
Mathieu Raffinot in 2003 in [5] ([4] contains further background). The algorithm and idea behind it will
be presented in the following with zero-indexing as opposed to one-indexing in [5]. Note that the original
algorithm only handles CBG patterns – not ECBG patterns.
A CBG pattern P can be used to build a simple non-deterministic automaton that can be used to
find all matches of P in a text T. The automaton has L + 1 states that can be numbered from 0 to L.
The initial state is state 0 and contains a self-loop that allows all letters (Σ). The loop is to allow that a
potential match may start at any position in T. The remaining automaton is build in the following way:
Set the current state s to 0. Iterate (from start to end) through the pattern P = P0, . . . , Pm−1. If Pj
is a character class [Cj] then create a transition from state s to s + 1 that permits all characters in Cj
and update the current state s ←s + 1. This models when a character matches one from the character
class [Cj]. Otherwise, Pj = x(aj, bj) is a bounded gap. Create transitions that permits any character
(Σ) between each successively numbered state from s to s + bj. If aj < bj, create ϵ-transitions from s to
all states numbered between s + 1 and s + bj −aj (both inclusive). Update the current state s ←s + bj.
The Σ-transitions ensures that bj characters can be matched as the gap. The ϵ-transitions allows to skip
bj −aj Σ-transitions which means that between aj and bj characters can match the gap. The final state
is state L and tells when a match have been found. Figure 2.2 shows the automaton that corresponds to
the pattern [a] −[ab] −x(2, 5) −[b] over Σ = {a, b, c}. The colors show which edges that are generated
by each pattern element Pj.
The active states can compactly be represented using a bit string of length L where the i’th bit is
set if state i + 1 in the non-deterministic automaton is active. State 0 is always active and therefore,
11
==PAGE:13==
Figure 2.2: The non-deterministic automaton generated from the CBG pattern [a] −[ab] −x(2, 5) −[b]
over Σ = {a, b, c}.
it is excluded. For simplicity, it is assumed that the bit string fits within a computer word of length w
(L ≤w). Inspired by the Shift-And algorithm [3], the edges going from state i to state i+1 for 0 ≤i ≤L
are simulated by shifting all bits once to the left while shifting in an 1 (from state 0) and then bitwise-and
with B[c], the bitmask for the current character c from the table B. B[c] has a 1 at position i if state
i + 1 has an ingoing transition that contains c. In Figure 2.2, B[a] = 011111112, B[b] = 111111102 and
B[c] = 011111002.
It is noted that all ϵ-transitions go from a state i representing the start of a gap x(aj, bj) to the
following bj −aj states. Hence, whenever state i is active then state i + 1 to i + bj −aj (both inclusive)
should be active too. As seen with setLSBs in section 2.6, subtraction can be used to set the k least
significant successive bits by subtracting 1 from 10k. setBits then shifts the result to set any k successive
bits, but this can also be achieved by shifting both operands rather than the result of the subtraction.
Hence, if state i (= bit at position i −1) represents the start of the gap x(aj, bj) and is active, then the
active states should be bitwise-or’ed with (10i+bj−aj) −(10i−1) = (1 << (i + bj −aj)) −(1 << (i −1)).
Note that this also sets the (i−1)’th bit that represents the (active) state representing the start of a gap.
Two bitmasks I (initial gap states) and F (final gap states) are created, where bits for the first operand
for all bounded gaps are set in F and bits for the second operand for all bounded gaps are set in I. At
this point, it is assumed that no gaps interfere in which case F −I then performs all the ϵ-transitions at
once as assumed in [5]. However, this is not always the case as will be discussed in section 5.1.
The active states are tracked in the bit string D. The active states that represent the start of gaps
then corresponds to D & I. To only perform the enabled ϵ-transitions, one can use F −(D & I), but
this might leave bits from F, since some bits in I may be cleared but their counterparts in F are not
cleared. A bit-pair is defined as the bit in I and the bit in F that are set by a bounded gap. For each
bit-pair, there are two possibilities: (1) Both bits in the bit-pair are set such that the subtraction set the
desired bits. Note that the bit in F is not set in the result. (2) Only the bit in F is set which leaves it in
the result. Hence, bitwise-and the result of the subtraction with ∼F will remove the undesired bits and
thus perform all enabled ϵ-transitions. Finally, set nF ←∼F and create a bitmask M ←10L−1 for the
final state. The whole algorithm is summed up in Listing 2.1. Note that there are some minor changes
compared to the one presented in [5]. In particular, the setBit function is used and the if-statement in
the loop is moved to the bottom since D = 0 and M ̸= 0L in the first iteration implies D & M = 0. It
uses O(L|Σ|) time for preprocessing and O(n) time for matching as stated in [5].
12
==PAGE:14==
/* Preprocessing */
I ←0L, F ←0L, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0L
i ←0
for j ∈0 . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
I ←setBit(I, i −1)
F ←setBit(F, i + bj −aj)
for k ∈i . . . i + bj −1, c ∈Σ do
B[c] ←setBit(B[c], k)
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj] then
for c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBit(B[c], k)
i ←i + 1
nF ←∼F
M ←setBit(0L, L −1)
/* Matching */
D ←0
for j ∈0 . . . n −1 do
// Perform state i to state i + 1 transitions.
D ←((D << 1) | 0L−11) & B[Tj]
// Perform ϵ-transitions.
D ←D | (F −(D & I)) & nF)
// Check for match.
if D & M ̸= 0L then
report match ending at j
Listing 2.1: Slightly modified version of the original main algorithm by Navarro and Raffinot [5] that
only handles CBG patterns satisfying L ≤w.
13
==PAGE:15==
3
Repeated (Negated) Character Classes
The main algorithm can easily be extended to also handle repeated and negated character classes just by
modifying the preprocessing to support ECBG patterns. A negated character class {Cj} is preprocessed
by setting the bit for all characters in the alphabet Σ and then clearing the bit for all c ∈Cj. A repeated
(negated) character class is handled by repeating the step for the (negated) character class the number
of times it is repeated. The changes in the preprocessing are shown in red in Listing 3.1.
I ←0L, F ←0L, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0L
i ←0
for j ∈0 . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
I ←setBit(I, i −1)
F ←setBit(F, i + bj −aj)
for k ∈i . . . i + bj −1, c ∈Σ do B[c] ←setBit(B[c], k)
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj](nj) then
for k ∈i . . . i + nj −1, c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBit(B[c], k)
i ←i + nj
if Pj is {Cj}(nj) then
for k ∈i . . . i + nj −1, c ∈Σ do B[c] ←setBit(B[c], k)
for k ∈i . . . i + nj −1, c ∈Cj do B[c] ←clearBit(B[c], k)
i ←i + nj
nF ←∼F
M ←setBit(0L, L −1)
Listing 3.1: Extended preprocessing of the main algorithm that can handle ECBG patterns with L ≤w.
Changes compared to Listing 2.1 are highlighted in red.
Initializing of all variables takes O(|Σ|) time. Each bounded gap x(aj, bj) sets one bit in I and F,
which takes O(1) time. A bounded gap also sets one bit, bj times per character in Σ which uses O(bj|Σ|)
time. Each repeated character class [Cj](nj) sets, one bit nj times per character in Cj ⊆Σ which uses
O(nj|Σ|) time. Each repeated negated character class {Cj}(nj) sets one bit per character in Σ and clears
one bit per character in Cj ⊂Σ, both nj times and takes O(nj|Σ|) time. Hence, the preprocessing time
is still O(L|Σ|). Note that the factors before |Σ| sums up to L (see section 2.3).
14
==PAGE:16==
4
Preprocessing Optimization
The preprocessing in the original algorithm and the extended algorithm to handle ECBG patterns are
far from optimal. This section presents optimizations to greatly reduce the time complexity.
The first observation is that all the double nested loops set one bit at a time for each of the specified
characters. This can be greatly improved by setting all successive bits at once using the setBits function
defined in section 2.6. The preprocessing time for bounded gaps can be improved further by collecting
all bits in a new bitmask G (gap bits) and then perform the bitwise-or operation after preprocessing the
whole pattern. Thus each bounded gap can be preprocessed in O(1) time by using O(|Σ|) extra time
once at the end.
The preprocessing time for a repeated character class with the usage of setBits is reduced to O(|Σ|)
or O(|Pj|) when Pj is a repeated character class. Note that |Pj| ≤|Σ|.
Currently, repeated negated character class use O(|Σ|) time with the usage of setBits. This can be
improved further by first introducing a new bitmask N (negated positions) and then set the bits in N
that was previously set in B[c] for all c ∈Σ. Next, instead of clearing the bits in B[c] for all c ∈Cj, those
bits are now set. Now, each negated repeated character class also uses O(|Pj|) time. After preprocessing
the whole pattern, bitwise-xor is performed on each B[c] with N to toggle bits in B[c]. Only positions
with 1-bits in N can change by this operation. If a bit is set in both B[c] and N then the result is a
cleared bit which correctly reflects that the character appeared in the corresponding repeated negated
character class. If a bit is set in N but not in B[c] then the result is a set bit which corresponds to c was
not in the corresponding repeated negated character class. This costs O(|Σ|) extra time once at the end.
Each element in the pattern now uses time propositional to their size such that the total time usage is
O(|P| + |Σ|). Further note that a ECBG pattern can be specified optimally meaning that for repeated
(negated) character classes Pj = [Cj](nj) or Pj = {Cj}(nj), then |Pj| −1 = |Cj| ≤|Σ|/2 by choosing the
smallest set of Cj and Σ \ Cj.
The updated preprocessing is presented in Listing 4.1. The variable nF is replaced with ∼F because,
as will be discussed in section 12.3, this can be faster.
The preprocessing time can be expected O(|P|) time.
It requires using a dynamic hash table [2]
where only the characters occurring in the pattern are initialized. N and G should then only be used on
elements present in the hash table. Usage of N and G can alternatively be moved to the matching phase
without affecting its time complexity but at the cost of two extra operations per character in T. The
space is still O(|Σ|) or O(|C|) if C ⊆Σ is the set of characters used in P. However, these are often bad
trade-offs in practice due to the extra operations using a dynamic hash table and/or moving operations
to the matching phase, since T is often much longer than P. The only exception is if Σ is much larger
than P and T.
15
==PAGE:17==
I ←0L, F ←0L, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0L
G ←0L, N ←0L
i ←0
for j ∈0 . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
I ←setBit(I, i −1)
F ←setBit(F, i + bj −aj)
G ←setBits(G, i, bj)
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj](nj) then
for c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBits(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
if Pj is {Cj}(nj) then
N ←setBits(N, i, nj)
for c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBits(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←(B[c] ⊕N) | G
// Removed: nF ←∼F
M ←setBit(0L, L −1)
Listing 4.1: Optimized preprocessing of the algorithm that can handle ECBG patterns with L ≤w.
Changes compared to Listing 3.1 are highlighted in red.
16
==PAGE:18==
5
Edge Cases
The original algorithm [5] and the optimized extended algorithm presented in section 4 does not handle
the following edge cases (from most to least obvious):
 Patterns that starts and/or ends with a bounded gap are not supported directly.
 Patterns with a bounded gap with minimum length zero or one followed by any bounded gap can
produce unexpected results.
 Patterns that contain x(0, i) −[C] −x(0, j) or x(0, i) −{C} −x(0, j) where i ≥1, j ≥1 and C is a
set of characters can produce unexpected results.
The following sections will be describe each problem in detail and show how to solve them. The problems
listed above will be handled in reverse order.
5.1
Conflicting Bits
Until now, it has been assumed that no bounded gaps interfere in regards to the subtraction F −(D &I).
However, this is not the case for some patterns like Pa = A −x(0, 1) −D −x(0, 1) −D. The automaton
that corresponds to Pa is shown in Figure 5.1. The automaton does not reveal any problems. Instead,
the algorithm is ran with the pattern Pa and the text T = ADD to illustrate the problem:
/* Preprocessing */
Σ = {A, D}, L = 5
I = 00101, F = 10100, M = 10000, B[A] = 01011, B[D] = 11110
/* Matching: First iteration. */
D ←00000
D ←((D << 1) | 00001) & B[A] = ((00000 << 1) | 00001) & 01011 = 00001
D ←D | ((F −(D & I)) & ∼F) = 00001 | ((10100 −00001) & 01011) = 00001 | (10011 & 01011) = 00011
D & M = 00011 & 10000 = ⊥
/* Matching: Second iteration. */
D ←((D << 1) | 00001) & B[A] = ((00011 << 1) | 00001) & 11110 = 00111 & 11110 = 00110
D ←D | ((F −(D & I)) & ∼F) = 00110 | ((10100 −00100) & 01011) = 00110 | (10000 & 01011) = 00110
D & M = 00110 & 10000 = ⊥
/* Matching: Third iteration. */
D ←((D << 1) | 00001) & B[A] = ((00110 << 1) | 00001) & 11110 = 01101 & 11110 = 01100
D ←D | ((F −(D & I)) & ∼F) = 01100 | ((10100 −00100) & 01011) = 01100 | (10000 & 01011) = 01100
D & M = 01100 & 10000 = ⊥
Listing 5.1: Run of the algorithm shown in Listing 4.1 with Pa and the text T = ADD. The red numbers
show the conflicting bits in F and D & I and the incorrect bits that follows.
The algorithm reports no matches, but it is clear to see that Pa matches T = ADD by choosing both
bounded gaps to be the empty string. As indicated by red, the same bit is set in both I and F which
17
==PAGE:19==
Figure 5.1: The non-deterministic automaton for the ECG pattern Pa = A −x(0, 1) −D −x(0, 1) −D.
should not be the case for it to work correctly. These bits will be called conflicting bits. The idea is that
I and F are used to generate 1-bits for the enabled ϵ-transitions. However, as illustrated above, this does
not always work when there are conflicting bits. In particular, whenever a state representing the start
of a gap is active and the bit at that position is set in both I and F then the bits cancel each other
out instead of generating 1-bits. Below is the subtraction shown for all possible values of D & I for this
particular example where the red numbers show where the opposite bit is expected:
F
−(D & I)
= F −(D & I)
& ∼F
= (F −(D & I)) & ∼F
10100
−00000
= 10100
& 01011
= 00000
10100
−00001
= 10011
& 01011
= 00011
10100
−00100
= 10000
& 01011
= 00000
10100
−00101
= 01111
& 01011
= 01011
There does not seem to be an obvious solution, but one makes an interesting observation if the algorithm
is ran with Pd = D −x(0, 1) −D −x(0, 1) −D and the text T = DDD: It finds a match even though I
and F are the same as above! The difference is that Pa triggers the second and third case above while Pd
triggers the second and forth case. The first case can never give problems since no 1-bits are generated
in that case. Hence, the third case must be the problem while the forth case does not result in a problem
even though it has one wrong bit! Recall that in section 2.7, it was noted that the 1-bit that triggers
ϵ-transitions is set again by the subtraction. Therefore, the unset bit in the fourth case actually does not
matter, since it is already set in D. However, this is not the case for the other wrong bit in the third
case.
The problem can be solved in a rather simple, but not obvious, way. One should just remove all the
active conflicting bits from F which can be accomplished by F ′ ←F & ∼(D & I). This will fix the third
case without affecting the others:
F ′
−(D & I)
= F ′ −(D & I)
& ∼F
= (F ′ −(D & I)) & ∼F
10100
−00000
= 10100
& 01011
= 00000
10100
−00001
= 10011
& 01011
= 00011
10000
−00100
= 01100
& 01011
= 01000
10000
−00101
= 01011
& 01011
= 01011
Hence, using two extra operations per character in T will fix this edge case. However, only very few
patterns will have this issue, so it is a high cost for an edge case, but this case was actually found in
patterns from the PROSITE database [28]. In the preprocessing phase, it could be checked if any bits
are conflicting and if not, the two extra operations could be avoided. However, there would have to be
18
==PAGE:20==
added an if-statement to the matching phase. Doing it inside the loop costs one or three extra operations
per iteration while outside would result in code duplication. Therefore, the two extra operations are just
kept. The time complexity (in any case) for the matching phase is unchanged.
5.2
Successive Bounded Gaps
The issue with successive bounded gaps is somewhat related to the previous issue. A pattern containing
x(1, b1)−x(a2, b2) that starts at state i will set the (i−1)’th bit in I, the (i+b1 −1)’th bit in F, increase
i by b1, then the next gap will set the (i + b1 −1)’th bit in I and finally set the (i + b1 + b2 −a2)’th bit
in F. Hence, this gives a conflicting bit in I and F at the (i + b1 −1)’th bit. This is the same problem
as in the previous section and thus is already solved.
A pattern containing x(0, b1)−x(a2, b2) that starts at state i will have the (i−1)’th and (i+b1 −1)’th
bit set in I while the (i + b1)’th and (i + b1 + b2 −a2)’th bit set in F. Hence, the two bits in I are both
at lower positions than both the corresponding bits in F. Recall that a bit-pair is the bit in I and the
bit in F that are set when preprocessing a bounded gap. For the algorithm to work, then no position of
1-bits in I and F may be within the positions of any bit-pairs. However, the current example contains
bits that violate that property. The problem that occurs when the property is violated is first of all that
the subtraction will not work as intended.
If the subtraction was assumed to work as intended, then the simulated automaton would still have
a problem in the way it is simulated.
Figure 5.2 shows the part of the automaton that arises from
x(0, b1)−x(a2, b2). State i+b1 has both ingoing and outgoing ϵ-transitions which means that two rounds
of ϵ-transitions simulation is needed. However, the algorithm only supports performing one round from
the active states. One option to fix this is to calculate the ϵ-closure and adjust the bits in I and F
according to that. A much simpler way to solve this problem is to combine all successive bounded gaps
into one gap. For simplicity of the algorithm, it will be assumed that this is always the case since there
is no reason to split the bounded gaps when specifying the pattern.
Figure 5.2: The part of the non-deterministic automaton that arises from the CBG pattern x(0, b1) −
x(a2, b2).
5.3
Bounded Gaps in the Start and End
The first observation is that a bounded gap in the start of a pattern would evaluate i −1 to −1 which
is out of bounds. Hence, the a gap in the start of a pattern should be handled differently. There are
two ways to handle it. The first option is to skip preprocessing of a bounded gap x(a0, b0) at the start
and instead skip the first a0 characters of T to ensure the gap can fit before any match. The second
option uses the observation that a bounded gap x(a0, b0) in the start effectively means that state 0 to
state b0 −a0 are always active. Hence, the gap could be preprocessed as normal with the exception that
neither I and F have bits set for this gap. In the matching phase, simulation of the normal transitions
would be changed to D ←((D << 1b0−a0+1) | 1b0−a0+1) & B[Tj].
19
==PAGE:21==
Similar, a bounded gap x(am−1, bm−1) at the end can be handled in two ways. The first option is to
skip the last am−1 characters of T to ensure space for the gap after any match. The second option is to
handle a gap at the end as a normal gap. However, if L = w and am−1 = 0 then for a gap in the end
i + bm−1 −am−1 = L −0 = L. This is out of bounds, but it does not matter for the simulation if this bit
is missing in F, since the subtraction will still underflow and thus set the needed bits.
If the solutions with skipping the start and/or end of T, then multiple matches may have been found
when a match is found. If a match with end position j is found with a pattern P that starts with a
bounded gap x(a0, b0), then a match with P without the first bounded gap is found. The bounded gap
will then only make the start position a0 to b0 smaller. Hence, no changes are needed since only the end
position is reported. Note that this removes a0 states from the automaton meaning that the final state
needs to be adjusted.
If a match with end position j is found with a pattern P that ends with a bounded gap x(am−1, bm−1),
then a match with P without the last bounded gap is found. The bounded gap is then added to the end,
meaning that all end positions from j + am−1 to j + bm−1 (both inclusive) should be reported.
Handling a bounded gap x(a0, b0) in the start is most effectively handled by skipping the first a0
characters since it skips a0 iterations in the matching phase and keep the constants in the matching
phase. A bounded gap x(am−1, bm−1) in the end may seem to be handled most efficient by skipping
the last am−1 characters of T. However, it requires adding a counter to report the extra matches and
adjust the end positions which adds more work per iteration and more variables. The other option just
changes the automaton in the preprocessing and thus is most efficient. These choices do not change the
complexities of the preprocessing and matching phases.
5.4
Updated Algorithm
Listing 5.2 shows the changes needed to handle all the edge cases. To keep it as compact as possible,
the conditional ternary operator (? :) will be used. Note that the (i −1)’th bit is set in M instead of the
(L −1)’th bit since L = start + i after the preprocessing loop.
20
==PAGE:22==
/* Preprocessing */
I ←0L, F ←0L, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0L
G ←0L, N ←0L
i ←0
start ←P0 is x(a0, b0) ? a0 : 0
for j ∈(P0 is x(a0, b0) ? 1 : 0) . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
I ←setBit(I, i −1)
if i + bj −aj < L then
F ←setBit(F, i + bj −aj)
G ←setBits(G, i, bj)
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj](nj) then
for c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBits(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
if Pj is {Cj}(nj) then
N ←setBits(N, i, nj)
for c ∈Cj do B[c] ←setBits(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←(B[c] ⊕N) | G
M ←setBit(0L, i −1)
/* Matching */
D ←0L
for j ∈start . . . n −1 do
// Perform state i to state i + 1 transitions.
D ←((D << 1) | 0L−11) & B[Tj]
// Perform ϵ-transitions.
cI ←D & I
cF ←F & ∼cI
D ←D | ((cF −cI) & ∼F)
// Check for match.
if D & M ̸= 0L then
report match ending at j
Listing 5.2: Algorithm with the fixes to handle the edge cases. Changes compared to Listing 4.1 (pre-
processing) and Listing 2.1 (matching) are highlighted in red.
21
==PAGE:23==
6
Longer Patterns
Until now, only patterns of max length L up to the size of a computer word w (64 bits in these days)
have been considered. Navarro and Raffinot [5] briefly mentions (in appendix) how to handle longer
patterns by using multiple words for representing states instead of a single word and how to perform
multiple words subtraction. However, only the bitwise operators (except bit shifts) are trivially extended
to multiple words and they leave out the details of their optimizations. The following sections describe
the changes needed for handling multiple words.
6.1
Preprocessing
The states are arrays of ⌈L/w⌉words. The i’th bit at position i is located in the array entry with index
⌊i/w⌋at the offset i % w. In case w = 2k where k is a (constant) positive integer, then the index and
offset can be calculated efficiently as:
index : int →int
offset : int →int
index(i) := i >> k
offset(i) := i & (w −1)
Setting, clearing and toggling a single bit as for example V ←setBit(V, i) is changed to V [index(i)] ←
setBit(V [index(i)], offset(i)).
However, setting multiple successive bits across multiple words is more
cumbersome, but can still be achieved efficiently by the following procedure:
procedure setBits ′(V : bitmask[], i : int, k : int)
idx ←index(i)
count ←min(w −offset(i), k)
V [idx] ←setBits(V [idx], offset(i), count)
idx ←idx + 1
left ←k −count
while left ≥w do
V [idx] ←1w
left ←left −w
idx ←idx + 1
if idx < ⌈L/w⌉then
V [idx] ←V [idx] | setLSBs(left)
Listing 6.1: Procedure for setting k successive bits starting at position i in a bitmask that consists of
multiple words. Note that the procedure does not return an updated bitmask array but performs the
changes directly on the given bitmask array (as a side effect).
First, the index of the first entry to modify is calculated using index(i). Next, it is calculated how
many bits that should be set in the first entry by taking into account how many bits there are left after
the given offset offset(i). The number of bits left is w −offset(i) and no more bits than that can be set,
so min(w −offset(i), k) is the number of bits to set in the first word V [idx]. Afterwards, the next entry is
considered and the number of bits left to set is calculated. The following words are set to 1w as long as
22
==PAGE:24==
more than w bits remain to be set. Finally, the remaining bits are set as the first in the following word
unless it is out of bounds. This must be the last word to modify since left < w at that point.
At most 2 + ⌊k/w⌋words are considered and thus the time is O(⌊k/w⌋+ 1). This procedure replaces
all setBits operations in the preprocessing. All remaining operations in the preprocessing are simply
extended to iterate over each word and perform the bitwise operation per word.
Initializing all the variables takes O(|Σ|⌈L/w⌉) time, since each state now takes up ⌈L/w⌉words
that are set to 0w. Only operations that need to write to multiple words will cost extra compared to
preprocessing single word states. If one gap writes to both the word at index i and i + 1 in G, then no
other bounded gap can write to both those words since the algorithm does not return to the word at
index i. Hence, counting all bounded gaps together, they can at most write to ⌈L/w⌉−1 extra words
which costs O(⌈L/w⌉) extra time once, so all bounded gaps use up to O(|P| + ⌈L/w⌉) time. All repeated
(negated) character classes have the same upper bound on how many times there can be written to
multiple successive indexes. However, they do it for |Pj| characters (and N in case of negated character
class) instead of doing it once. Therefore, they use up to O(|P|⌈L/w⌉) extra time once, so they use up
to O(|P|⌈L/w⌉) time. The part after the whole pattern performs bitwise operations in a loop and thus
now uses O(|Σ|⌈L/w⌉) time. Hence, the total time usage is O((|P| + |Σ|)⌈L/w⌉).
The updated preprocessing to handle multiple words are shown in Listing 6.2.
I ←0L, F ←0L, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0L
G ←0L, N ←0L
i ←0
start ←P0 is x(a0, b0) ? a0 : 0
for j ∈(P0 is x(a0, b0) ? 1 : 0) . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
I[index(i −1)] ←setBit(I[index(i −1)], offset(i −1))
if i + bj −aj < L then
F[index(i + bj −aj)] ←setBit(F[index(i + bj −aj)], offset(i + bj −aj))
setBits′(G, i, bj)
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj](nj) then
for c ∈Cj do setBits′(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
if Pj is {Cj}(nj) then
setBits′(N, i, nj)
for c ∈Cj do setBits′(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
for c ∈Σ, k ∈0 . . . index(i −1) do B[c][k] ←(B[c][k] ⊕N[k]) | G[k]
M ←setBit(0w, offset(i −1))
Listing 6.2: Preprocessing of the algorithm that can handle any ECBG pattern by using multiple words
(arrays). Changes compared to Listing 5.2 are highlighted in red.
23
==PAGE:25==
6.2
Matching
Using multiple words in the matching phase simply extends all operations to be performed word-wise
in a loop while keeping track of the carry from the left bit shift by one and the subtraction. The carry
for left bit shift starts as 1 for the first entry (idx = 0) and is then D[idx] >> (w −1) for the entry
at index idx + 1. The carry for subtraction starts as 0 for idx = 0. For the following entry at index
idx + 1, the carry is 1 if the first operand (cF ←F[idx] & ∼cI) of the subtraction is less than the second
operand (cI ←D[idx] & I[idx]) with the carry (cI + carry) and otherwise the carry is 0. Raffinot and
Navarro [5] also mention that the special case where cI = 1w needs to be handled, since the carry can
make cI overflow if the carry is 1 such that the comparison above (cF < cI + carry) is false. Note that
cI = D[idx] & I[idx] = 1w implies that I[idx] = 1. However, there is actually no need to deal with this
special case if the assumption presented in section 5.2 is used. It says that a pattern may not contain
any successive bounded gaps. The reason is that the only way to get I[idx] = 1w is to use a pattern
that contains w successive gaps that are either x(0, 1) or x(1). If you use any other bounded gap (except
the meaningless x(0)) or a repeated (negated) character class, then you will skip at least one position
meaning that I[idx] = 1w is only possible for w = 1 (which would report all end positions). Furthermore
as discussed in section 5.2, the automaton resulting from this kind of pattern would also require w rounds
of ϵ-transitions whereas the algorithm only supports one. Hence, at least two operations (a comparison
and logical-or with the other comparison) can be removed.
Finally, the if guard D & M can be optimized by only checking the entry where the bit in M is
present, namely the index(i −1)’th entry, since all other entries result in 0w. The whole matching phase
uses O(n⌈L/w⌉) time with multiple words, since each character requires iterating through states of size
O(⌈L/w⌉). Listing 6.3 shows the matching loop when using multiple words.
D ←0L
for j ∈start . . . n −1 do
shiftCarry ←1, subCarry ←0
for k ∈0 . . . index(i −1) do
// Perform state i to state i + 1 transitions.
nextShiftCarry ←D[k] >> (w −1)
D[k] ←((D[k] << 1) | shiftCarry) & B[Tj][k]
shiftCarry ←nextShiftCarry
// Perform ϵ-transitions.
cI ←D[k] & I[k]
cF ←F[k] & ∼cI
D[k] ←D[k] | ((cF −cI−subCarry) & ∼F[k])
subCarry ←cF < cI + subCarry
// Check for match.
if D[index(i −1)] & M ̸= 0w then
report match ending at j
Listing 6.3: Matching of the algorithm that can handle any ECBG pattern by using multiple words
(arrays). Changes compared to Listing 5.2 are highlighted in red.
24
==PAGE:26==
7
Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD)
Currently, only patterns up to maximum length w = 64 can be processed efficiently. Longer patterns
have to use multiple words which come with a large overhead of having loops. In addition, it requires
more loads and stores unless loops are unrolled. This requires that the number of words is known at
compile time or that specialized code for each possible (needed) number of words is compiled. Both these
approaches have huge downsides. In practice, almost all PROSITE patterns fits within 64-bit words, but
a few patterns requires more bits [28]. The longest (as of now) has a maximum length of 123. Modern
CPUs offers Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) or vector instructions that allows to perform the
same operation on multiple words (a vector) with a single instruction. The remaining part of this section
will present the model of SIMD that will be used when designing algorithms in SIMD. The model will then
be used in section 8 to present how one can efficiently simulate longer patterns using SIMD. Section 9 will
use a similar approach to section 6 to allow any pattern length. Finally, section 10 looks at the perhaps
more intuitive usage of SIMD, namely to use SIMD to simulate the automaton in parallel.
7.1
Model
The SIMD-model consists of vectors with the type vec. Each vector consists of exactly X vector elements.
Each vector element is an (unsigned) w-bit integer. Hence, a vector may be used to represent X w-bit
integers or a (X ∗w)-bit integer. When writing up a vector, the first element (index 0) is the right-most
element while the last element (index X −1) is the left-most element. Hence, this is the same direction as
when one writes the bits of in an integer from the least significant (right-most) bit to the most significant
bit (left-most). This might seem counter-intuitive when writing the definition of vector operations, but
gives the advantage that the concatenation of bits from all elements will give the binary representation
of an (X ∗w)-bit integer. Thus, it allows us to write a bit repetition pattern to specify the value of a
whole vector, for instance 1X∗w is the vector of X elements with the value 1w.
The definitions of the vectorized infix operators and some vector specific functions are given in List-
ing 7.1. The conditional ternary operator (? :) and msb are used for a compact representation.
c
opa : vec × vec →vec
A c
opa B := [AX−1 opa BX−1, . . . , A0 opa B0]
for opa ∈{+, −, &, |, ⊕}
c
opr : vec × vec →vec
A c
opr B := [AX−1 opr BX−1 ? 1w : 0w, . . . ]
for opr ∈{=, ̸=, <, ≥, >, ≤}
b∼: vec →vec
b∼A := [∼AX−1, . . . , ∼A0]
c
<< : vec × int →vec
A c
<< i := [AX−1 << i, . . . , A0 << i]
c
>> : vec × int →vec
A c
>> i := [AX−1 >> i, . . . , A0 >> i]
c
<< : vec × vec →vec
A c
<< B := [AX−1 << BX−1, . . . , A0 << B0]
c
>> : vec × vec →vec
A c
>> B := [AX−1 >> BX−1, . . . , A0 >> B0]
broadcast : int →vec
broadcast(i) := [i, . . . , i]
shuffle : vec × vec →vec
shuffle[iX−1, . . . , i0](A) := [AiX−1, . . . , Ai0]
blend : vec × vec × vec →vec
blend[iX−1, . . . , i0](A, B) := [iX−1 ? BX−1 : AX−1, . . . , i0 ? B0 : A0]
msbs : vec →int
msbs(A) := (msb(AX−1) << (X −1)) | · · · | (msb(A0) << 0)
mask2vec : int →vec
mask2vec(i) := [(i & 2X−1) ̸= 0w ? 1w : 0w, . . . , (i & 20) ̸= 0w ? 1w : 0w]
Listing 7.1: Overview of infix operators and functions defined in the SIMD model.
25
==PAGE:27==
The hat (b) over an operator indicates that it is the vector version.
Note that the bit shifts are
overloaded to accept an integer or a vector. broadcast creates a vector where all elements are the same.
shuffle generates a vector from the elements in the vector A by selecting the elements specified in a vector
of indexes ([iX−1, . . . , i0]). blend interleaves elements from two vectors based on a bitmask (or vector).
msbs extracts the most significant bit (sign bit) of each vector element into a X-bit bitmask. mask2vec
transforms a X-bit bitmask into a vector where the i’th element is 1w if the i’th bit is set and otherwise
0w.
26
==PAGE:28==
8
Longer Patterns using SIMD
This section shows how SIMD can be used to support patterns with a max length up to L ≤X ∗w.
8.1
Preprocessing
The preprocessing cannot really utilize SIMD since the preprocessing would need to be able to set different
bits in each element in the vector. SIMD is optimized for performing the same operation on each element
which is not the case in the preprocessing. Therefore, the vectors are preprocessed like they were arrays
of X words. The only exceptions are the initialization and the final loop in the preprocessing. The SIMD
operation V b⊕V can be used to initialize all the vectors to 0X∗w since bitwise-xor with the same value
will have the same bits set and thus result in 0X∗w. The final loop performs B[c] ←(B[c] ⊕N) | G. Here,
SIMD is also useful because it has to be done for all vectors in the B and SIMD allows to handle one
entry in B in one operation instead of X operations. With these optimizations, the ⌈L/w⌉factor can be
removed from |Σ| such that the preprocessing is improved to O(|P|⌈L/w⌉+ |Σ|) time.
8.2
Matching
The matching phase has much better potential for utilizing SIMD. However, the challenge is to use SIMD
to operate on unsigned (X ∗w)-bit integers using operations that operate on X w-bit words. The bitwise
operators except bit shifts (b∼, b&, b| and b⊕) work out of the box. Hence, only left shift by one, (X ∗w)-bit
integer subtraction and checking for a match are missing.
Checking for a matches can be done using msbs((D b&M) b̸=0X∗w) ̸= 0. (D b&M) b̸=0X∗w is simply the
vectorized version of the check that returns the results (either 0w or 1w) for the individual elements as a
vector. Hence, msbs can extract the sign bit from each element to get a X-bit bitmask. If the bitmask
contains a 1-bit then the element with the 1-bit in M was matched and there is a match. Checking for a
1-bit is the same as checking that the bitmask is not zero. Clearly, this operation uses constant O(1) time.
Alternatively, the element where M has the 1-bit could be compared with the corresponding element in D
by extracting them. However, the model does not cover extraction of elements from a vector. Therefore,
it will not be considered as an option.
The following sections will describe how left shift by one can be performed in constant time and how
(X ∗w)-bit integer subtraction can be performed in O(X) time. Later, two constant time alternatives
for subtraction are presented.
8.2.1
Left Shift by One
The following procedure shows how to left shift a (X ∗w)-bit integer by 1 using SIMD:
procedure shl1(V : vec) : vec // V << 1
Carries ←V c
>> (w −1)
RotatedCarries ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Carries)
ShiftedCarries ←RotatedCarries b& (1X∗w−10)
Shifted ←V c
<< 1
return Shiftedb| ShiftedCarries
Listing 8.1: SIMD procedure to shift a vector one bit to the left when viewed as an (X ∗w)-bit integer.
27
==PAGE:29==
First, the most significant bit in each vector element is extracted by right shifting each vector element
by w −1 bits as these are the Carries.
Next, all carries are rotated one element forward to move
the carries to the next element using shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1]. It works by having i −1
as index for the i’th element except for the 0th that has X −1 because it does not have a previous
element. Note that this moves the carry from the last ((X −1)’th) element to the first (0th) element
and thus potentially moves a 1-carry to where there should never be a carry. Therefore, bitwise-and
with 1X∗w−10 is needed to remove the potential 1-bit to get the ShiftedCarries. Afterwards, all vector
elements are shifted one bit to the left (Shifted). The result is then the bitwise-or between the Shifted
vector elements and the ShiftedCarries. This implements V << 1 for (X ∗w)-bit integers in 5 SIMD
operations.
shl1(V ) b| (0X∗w−11) can then be calculated using one extra operation.
However, it can
also be calculated in 5 SIMD operations by replacing ShiftedCarries ←Carries b& (1X∗w−10) with
ShiftedCarries ←Carriesb| (0X∗w−11) such that instead of removing an eventual carry from the last
element (in the first element), the bit is always set such that the final bitwise-or indirectly performs the
bitwise-or with 0X∗w−11. This alternative version of shl1 will be called shl1or1 and will be used from
now on. The procedure does not depend on the number of vector elements and thus uses constant time.
It is also possible to calculate V <<1 as V +V but as will be seen in the following sections, subtraction
(and thus also addition) is considerably slower when operation on vectors as arbitrary (X∗w)-bit integers.
8.2.2
Basic Subtraction
The following procedure shows how Basic Subtraction between two (X ∗w)-bit integers can be performed
using SIMD by calculating the carries element by element:
procedure subbsc(A : vec, B : vec) : vec // A −B
GT ←B b> A
EQ ←A b= B
Carries ←0X∗w // 0th carry.
Temp ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](GT)
Pos ←[0, . . . , 0, 1, 0]
Carries ←blend[Pos](Carries, Temp) // 1st carry.
for j ∈2 . . . X −1 do
Temp ←EQ b& Temp
Temp ←GT b| Temp
Temp ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Temp)
Pos ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Pos)
Carries ←blend[Pos](Carries, Temp) // j’th carry.
return (A b−B) b+ Carries
Listing 8.2: SIMD procedure to subtract two arbitrary vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers using Basic Sub-
traction that calculates the carries one by one.
The function simply calculates the carries using SIMD as one could do with only one word. It first
checks if the second operand is larger than the first (GT) and if they are equal (EQ). The i’th element
28
==PAGE:30==
should generate a carry for the (i + 1)’th element if GTi = 1w or if the (i −1)’th element generated
a carry for the i’th element and EQi = 1w.
Note that if EQi = 1w then Ai −Bi = 0w and thus
a carry will trigger an overflow that results in a carry to the next element. Hence, the carry for the
i’th element can recursively be calculated as: Carryi = GTi−1 | (EQi−1 & Carryi−1). The carry for
the first element is Carry0 = 0. Hence, the Carries can initially be set to 0X∗w. The second carry
is Carry1 = GT0 | (EQ0 & 0X∗w) = GT0. To get the carry to the right position, the elements in GT
are rotated one element forward, like in shl1, such that Temp1 ←GT0. Now the second carry can be
saved in Carries by using blend[0, . . . , 0, 1, 0](Carries, Temp) to insert the second element from Temp
into Carries. For the remaining, there is no simplification of the recursive formula. When entering
the loop, note that Tempj−1 = Carryj−1. Bitwise-and with EQ followed by bitwise-or with GT gives
Tempj−1 = GTj−1 & (EQj−1 | Carryj−1).
It is needed at position j, so the elements in Temp and
Pos are rotated one element forward such that Tempj = GTj−1 & (EQj−1 | Carryj−1) and Posj = 1.
Afterwards, the next Carryj can be inserted into Carries using blend[Pos](Carries, Temp). Finally,
after calculating all carries, the actual subtraction can be made where the carries are added instead of
subtracted, because the carries are 1w = −1 and not 1! It uses up to 5 SIMD operations per element
plus 4 SIMD operations. There are X elements and thus it uses O(X) time.
Returning to the algorithm, ⌈L/w⌉vector elements are needed meaning that the loop could be stopped
when ⌈L/w⌉elements have been processed. Assuming ⌈L/w⌉≤X, it uses O(⌈L/w⌉) time. Thus, there
is no gain compared to using normal instructions except this avoid switching between normal registers
and SIMD registers via memory.
8.2.3
Thorup Subtraction
Thorup [6] presented an efficient way to calculate the carries for vector addition assuming X ≤w.
Adapting it to subtraction, one can obtain an efficient way to calculate the carries for vector subtraction.
This way to perform subtraction will be called Thorup Subtraction:
procedure subthr(A : vec, B : vec) : vec // A −B
GT ←B b> A
EQ ←A b= B
gt ←msbs(GT)
eq ←msbs(EQ)
packedCarries ←((gt << 1) + eq) ⊕eq
Carries ←mask2vec(packedCarries)
return (A b−B) b+ Carries
Listing 8.3: SIMD procedure to subtract two arbitrary vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers using Thorup
Subtraction that calculates all carries in one go using non-vector operations.
First, it calculates GT and EQ as for Basic Subtraction above, but it then extracts the most significant
(MSB) bit from each vector element into a bitmask. Recall that GTi = 1w when Bi > Ai and EQi = 1w
when Ai = Bi. Thus the MSB is set if the comparison holds. Now, the carries could be calculated using
normal instructions with the recursive formula: Carryi = GTi−1 |(EQi−1 &Carryi−1). However, Thorup
[6] presented that all carries can be calculated in one go using ((gt<<1)+eq)⊕eq. It is clear that gt<<1
moves all the certain carries to the next position, but is not clear how addition and bitwise-xor with eq
29
==PAGE:31==
ends up giving the carries. Therefore, all four cases are considered:
gt << 1
+ eq
= (gt << 1) + eq
⊕eq
= ((gt << 1) + eq) ⊕eq
0
+ 0
= 0
⊕0
= 0
1
+ 0
= 1
⊕0
= 1
0
+ 1
= 1
⊕1
= 0
00 . . . 01
+ 01 . . . 11
= 10 . . . 00
⊕01 . . . 11
= 11 . . . 11
In the first case, gt << 1 and eq have 0-bits at position i which generates no carries as expected. In the
second case, gt << 1 has a 1-bit and eq has a 0-bit at position i which correctly generates a carry for the
current element. In the third case, gt << 1 has a 0-bit and eq has a 1-bit at position i which results in no
carries. None of these cases affects the carries for the following elements and thus only have local effect,
which is why only one column of bits is shown. However, the opposite is the case in the fourth case.
Here, both gt << 1 and eq have 1-bits at position i which gives a carry for the next bit such that the bits
at position i + 1 also need to be considered. Note that gt & eq = 0 since b > a =⇒a ̸= b. This means
that gt << 1 can only have a 0-bit at position i + 1 because eq had a 1-bit at position i. eq can have any
bit at position i+1. If eq has a 1-bit a position i+1, then the addition will again give a carry to the next
bit and thus we have the same case for the next bit. If eq has a 0-bit a position i + 1 then the addition
will generate no more carries to the following bits and thus not affect more bits. Summed up, gt << 1
moves all the certain carries to the next position. Adding gt << 1 to eq flips all bits in eq that receives
a carry from the previous bit. Note that the internal carriers when performing addition simulates the
(recursive) propagation of carries to the next element. The flipped bits are those that represent carries
and only those bits should be set. Using bitwise-xor with eq will clear all bits that have not been flipped
since they are the same as in eq and it will set all flipped bits since they are different compared to eq.
With the carries compactly represented in packedCarries, all that remains is to unpack them to vector
elements using mask2vec and calculate the actual subtraction like before. Note that unpacking the bits
sets an element to 1w or 0.
The procedure uses 7 SIMD operations and 3 normal operations assuming X ≤w. It does not depend
on the number of elements in the vector and thus uses constant O(1) time.
8.2.4
Specialized Subtraction
Both of the previous subtraction procedures perform subtraction of two arbitrary integers. The main
challenge was to efficiently calculate the carries because the carry for the last element depends on the
carries from all the previous elements. One can exploit the bit-pairs in I and F to pre-calculate which
bit in each element of the vector I that generates a carry and in that case which elements that get that
carry.
The first observation is that whenever a bit at position i is set in I during the preprocessing, then
a bit at position j > i is set in F. As previously mentioned in section 5.2, no other bits are set within
the positions i and j. An example will be used to illustrate how the bit patterns of I and F look. The
example will use a vector of four 8-bit elements where the most significant bit, when reading the vector
as an 32-bit integer, is the left-most bit:
F = 00010000
00000000
00001000
10000100
I = 00000000
00000000
00010010
00001001
The variables represent four bounded gaps where the bit-pairs are:
(I0, F2), (I3, F7), (I9, F11) and
(I12, F28).
Recall that the purpose of using subtraction is to set multiple successive bits in parallel.
30
==PAGE:32==
If I0 is set, then the bits 0 and 1 should be set. If I3 is set, then the bits 3 to 6 should be set. If I9 is set,
then the bits 9 to 10 should be set. If I12 is set, then the bits 12 to 27 should be set. It is noted that only
the last bit-pair set bits that span more than one 8-bit word. In that case, the subtraction in the first
8-bit word of the bit-pair underflows and generates a carry for the next element. All elements between
the first and the last element of the bit-pair, the middle-elements, in both operands cannot have any
bits set due to the property described above. Hence, the result for those elements will be 0w. However,
the first middle-element gets a carry that will make it underflow and thus generate a carry for the next
element. Hence, the result for all middle-elements is 1w and they will propagate the carry to the last
element.
The idea is then that you in the preprocessing can check if the bit in I and F are set in the same
element. If not, then you know that the carry generated from the element where the bit in I was set
should be propagated to all the following elements up to and including the element where the bit in F
was set. Returning to the example, the first two bit-pairs set both bits in the first elements and thus do
not generate any carries. Similar, the third bit-par only sets bits in the second element. The last bit-pair
sets a bit in the second element in I and a bit in the forth element in F. Hence, the third and forth
element should have a carry. Define C as the carries:
C = 00000001
00000001
00000000
00000000
Then calculate the 32-bit subtraction F −I by performing 8-bit subtractions as F −I −C:
F = 00010000
00000000
00001000
10000100
−
I = 00000000
00000000
00010010
00001001
−
C = 00000001
00000001
00000000
00000000
=
00001111
11111111
11110110
01111011
Recall that the actual subtraction is (F & ∼(I & D)) −(I & D). The above only covers the case where
F &I = 0 and D &I = I, which effectively simplifies the subtraction to F −I. This will now be extended
to handle F −(I & D).
Recall that the left over bits are removed after the subtraction by using bitwise-and with ∼F and
thus the left over bits from F do not have to be handled. Performing bitwise-and with D can only remove
bits in I. If one of the bits in I that previously generated a carry is removed, then the carry from that
element is going to be zero. The reason is that if any other bit in that element is set in I then there
is a bit in F in the same element at a higher position that prevents an underflow due to the properties
of bit-pairs. For instance, if the blue 1-bit in I in the example above is cleared then the next set bit in
the same element in I is the green 1-bit. However, F has a green 1-bit in the same element at a higher
position which prevents an underflow if the blue 1-bit in I is cleared. Hence, the carry is one when the
(blue) underflow bit is set and zero otherwise.
Define the variable S (shuffle indexes) that contains the index where each element gets its carry from
as described above. Each element defaults to get the carry from the previous element. In addition, define
the variable U (underflow bits) that contains the underflow bit for those elements that can underflow and
is otherwise zero. In the example, the variables will have the following values:
S =
1
1
0
3
U = 00000000
00000000
00010000
00000000
31
==PAGE:33==
Note that the first element is set to point at the last element because the last element can never underflow
since the MSB set in F is at a higher position than the MSB set in I. Note that in case the last bit to
set in F would be out of bounds, then UX−1 is still set to 0w.
It is now possible to define the initial version of what will be called Specialized Subtraction:
procedure subspz(A : vec, B : vec, S : vec, U : vec) : vec // A −B
Underflows ←B b& U
GT ←Underflows b̸= 0X∗w // Or use B b> A
Carries ←shuffle[S](GT)
return (A b−B) b+ Carries
Listing 8.4: SIMD procedure to subtract two vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers using the initial version of
Specialized Subtraction where the carry generating bits and carry destinations are known beforehand.
Note that the second version in Listing 8.5 uses fewer operations.
First, the bits that trigger underflow in B = cI = D & I are isolated using bitwise-and with U. This
either leaves 0w or a 1-bit at some position in each vector element. In order to turn the single 1-bit at any
position into 1w, each element is compared with 0w and if they differ, they are set to 1w and otherwise
stays as 0w. This effectively calculates the same as GT in Basic Subtraction, so one could just calculate
GT as B b>A. However, using U might actually be faster in some cases as discussed in section 13.6. After
calculating GT, shuffle[S] is used to move the carries directly to the needed elements. Finally, the actual
subtraction with the carries are performed.
Later on, it was discovered that above approach could be altered a bit to calculate GT in one operation
without any comparisons. Instead of generating a vector with bitmasks where the underflow bits are set
(U), a vector O with the offsets of the underflow bits can be generated. Hence, if Ui = 00010000 then
Oi = 4. In case Ui = 0 then Oi = w. The idea is now that instead of checking if the underflow bit is
present in B = cF = D & I, the bit is simply shifted to the least significant bit such that it gives 0w−11
if the bit is set and otherwise 0w. This is the case since the underflow bit is the most significant bit that
can be set in every element of I. Hence, the procedure can be changed to the following, where it should
be noted that the carries now are 0w−11 or 0w meaning they should be subtracted:
procedure subspz(A : vec, B : vec, S : vec, O : vec) : vec // A −B
GT ←B c
>> O
Carries ←shuffle[S](GT)
return (A b−B) b−Carries
Listing 8.5: SIMD procedure to subtract two vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers using Specialized Subtraction
where the carry generating bits and carry destinations are known beforehand.
This subtraction only uses 4 (or 5) SIMD operations! Hence, it provides a way to perform subtraction
in O(1) and is a huge improvement compared to previous methods for this special case since all operations
can be performed in SIMD registers.
However, the consequences of replacing F with F ′ = F & ∼(I & D) have not been considered. As
previously described, it ensures that bits in I and F does not conflict by removing the conflicting bits in
32
==PAGE:34==
F. The only cases of interest are the cases where the conflicting bit is part of a bit-pair that spans multiple
words, since all other cases within a single word have previously been shown to work. An example is:
F = 00000101
00010000
I = 00000001
00010001
If the conflicting bit is the most significant set bit in an element in both I and F, then it can generate a
carry if it is set in D & I. The generation of the carry is independent of conflicting bits because the bit
in F belongs to the previous bit-pair compared to the bit-pair that the bit in I is part of. Thus the bit
in I should still generate a carry if set which is the case. If the conflicting bit is the least significant set
bit in an element in both I and F, then there are two cases. If the element does not get a carry, then it
works as previously shown because the conflicting bit is within a single element that is not affected by
other elements. If it gets a carry then it is the carry to generate bits before the conflicting bit. In this
case, the carry acts as the 1-bit in I that forms the bit pair with the conflicting bit in F which is exactly
the single element case. However, the carry might clear the LSB in an element if the conflicting bit is at
offset 0. This does not matter since it would later be cleared by bitwise-and with ∼F. Hence, the above
subtraction procedure also works for (F & ∼(I & D)) −(I & D) without any further modifications.
As a last note, the changes needed in the preprocessing compared to the multiple words version for
calculating S, and O are shown below in red. In addition, the other changes related to the using SIMD
vectors instead of arrays are shown. This includes overloading the procedure setBits′ to accept a vector
(which can be seen as an array) in place of an array of words. Note that the vectors are preprocessed
as arrays of X words that then are used as vectors anywhere except in the preprocessing of the pattern.
This is to keep things more in line with the actual implementation. The changes do not affect the time
complexity of the preprocessing since the new vectors can be set/loaded in constant time and at most
⌈L/w⌉entries in S and O are changed during the preprocessing.
33
==PAGE:35==
/* Preprocessing */
I ←0X∗w, F ←0X∗w, M ←0X∗w, for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←0X∗w
G ←0X∗w, N ←0X∗w, S ←[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1], O ←broadcast(w)
i ←0
start ←P0 is x(a0, b0) ? a0 : 0
for j ∈(P0 is x(a0, b0) ? 1 : 0) . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
initialIndex ←index(i −1)
finalIndex ←index(i + bj −aj)
I[initialIndex] ←setBit(I[initialIndex], offset(i −1))
if i + bj −aj < L then
F[finalIndex] ←setBit(F[finalIndex], offset(i + bj −aj))
else // i + bj −aj = L (out of bounds).
finalIndex ←finalIndex −1
setBits′(G, i, bj)
if initialIndex < finalIndex then
O[initialIndex] ←offset(i −1)
for k ∈initialIndex + 1 . . . finalIndex do S[k] ←initialIndex
i ←i + bj
if Pj is [Cj](nj) then
for c ∈Cj do setBits′(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
if Pj is {Cj}(nj) then
setBits′(N, i, nj)
for c ∈Cj do setBits′(B[c], i, nj)
i ←i + nj
for c ∈Σ do B[c] ←(B[c] b⊕N)b| G
M[index(i −1)] ←setBit(0w, offset(i −1))
Listing 8.6: Vector version of the preprocessing. Changes compared to Listing 6.2 are highlighted in red.
34
==PAGE:36==
9
Even Longer Patterns using SIMD
Similar to section 6, it is possible to use multiple vectors to support even longer patterns in case L > X∗w.
This again requires iterating over each vector to perform the operators on each vector. Hence, there need
to be kept track of the carries between vectors for left shift by one and subtraction. These carries should
not be confused with the carries within a vector that are handled in section 8.
9.1
Preprocessing
When multiple vectors are used, then setting one bit in the preprocessing first requires finding the correct
vector, then the correct element in that vector before setting the bit at the correct offset. This makes
it very cumbersome (for the reader) and does not reflect how things are done in practice. Therefore,
multiple vectors will be preprocessed exactly as multiple words using arrays – except the initialization
and the final loop in the preprocessing that modifies the table B as these now loop over ⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉
vectors. The reason for this choice is that this allows a less complicated preprocessing and the data can
then be loaded as vectors in the matching phase. Each bitmask variable (except M) will then be an array
of ⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉vectors which corresponds to an array of X ∗⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉words. When viewing the array
as an array of words, the i’th vector can be loaded by loading the entries between i ∗X to (i + 1) ∗X −1
(both inclusive). M does not need to be an array, since only the vector where the 1-bit is set needs to be
saved. Yet again, the SIMD operation V b⊕V can be used (⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉times) to initialize all the vectors
to 0X∗w and the final loop can utilize the SIMD operations. Eventually see Listing 9.6 for the updated
preprocessing.
Only the initialization of variables and the final loop in the preprocessing that modifies the table B
affect the time complexity compared to the version using one vector that uses O(|P|⌈L/w⌉+ |Σ|) time.
Modifications adds the factor ⌈L/(Xw)⌉to the |Σ| part such that the time complexity is O(|P|⌈L/w⌉+
|Σ|⌈L/(Xw)⌉).
9.2
Matching
With multiple vectors, an inner loop like in the multiple words version (section 6.2) is needed to process
each vector with an additional carry. A sketch of the matching loop with the inner loop is shown in
Listing 9.1 where double brackets [[k]] accesses the k’th vector. The left shift by one bit and subtraction
carries are defined as vectors. However, the actual representation of the subtraction carry differs depend-
ing on the chosen subtraction method. shl1or1′ and sub′ represents the carry variant of the left shift
by one bit and subtraction procedures presented in section 8.2 that will be presented in the following
sections. The carry variants will besides the result also return the carry to the next vector. Also note
that indexX(i) calculate the index for the vector (not word) containing the bit at position i meaning
indexX(i) = ⌊index(i)/X⌋.
35
==PAGE:37==
for k ∈0 . . . indexX(i −1) do D[[k]] ←0X∗w
for j ∈start . . . n −1 do
ShiftCarry ←0X∗w−11, SubCarry ←0X∗w
for k ∈0 . . . indexX(i −1) do
// Perform state i to state i + 1 transitions.
Shifted, ShiftCarry ←shl1or1′(D[[k]], ShiftCarry)
D[[k]] ←Shifted b& B[Tj][[k]]
// Perform ϵ-transitions.
cI ←D[[k]] b& I[[k]]
cF ←F[[k]] b& b∼cI
Diff, SubCarry ←sub′(cF, cI, SubCarry)
D[[k]] ←D[[k]]b| (Diff b& b∼F[[k]])
// Check for match.
match ←msbs((D[[indexX(i −1)]] b& M) b̸= 0X∗w)
if match ̸= 0 then
report match ending at j
Listing 9.1: Multiple vectors version of the matching phase. Single brackets [k] accesses the k’th word
while double brackets [[k]] accesses the k’th vector. Changes compared to Listing 6.3 are highlighted in
red.
9.2.1
Left Shift by One
For the carry variant of the left shift by one, the version that includes the bitwise-or with 0X∗w−11 as a
part of the operation, namely shl1or1, is considered. The reason is that 0X∗w−11 can be seen as the carry
from the previous (non-existing) vector that represents state 0 in the automaton. Furthermore, blend
can then be used to insert the carry from the previous vector. At the same time, this avoids the problem
from rotating the carry from the last element to the first element since it is overwritten by the blend
operation. This results in the following procedure where the changes compared to the version without a
carry are shown in red:
procedure shl1or1′(V : vec, Carry : vec) : vec × vec // (V << 1) | Carry
Carries ←V c
>> (w −1)
RotatedCarries ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Carries)
ShiftedCarries ←blend[0, . . . , 0, 1](RotatedCarries, Carry)
Shifted ←V c
<< 1
return Shiftedb| ShiftedCarries, RotatedCarries
Listing 9.2: SIMD procedure to shift a vector one bit to the left and bitwise-or with a carry when the
vector is viewed as an (X ∗w)-bit integer.
36
==PAGE:38==
First of all, there is an additional argument Carry that contains the carry from the previous vector.
Then the Carries within the current vector are calculated and rotated like before. The new part is that
the Carry is inserted as the carry for the first element. For the first vector, the carry for the first element
is 0w−11 like before since Carry = 0X∗w−11 for the first vector. The result is the bitwise-or between all
vector elements shifted one bit to the left (Shifted) and the ShiftedCarries as before.
RotatedCarries is returned as the Carry for the next vector. RotatedCarries is chosen since it con-
tains the carry from the last element (the one for the next vector) in the first element of RotatedCarries.
Thus, RotatedCarries can be given directly to the call of shl1or1 for the next vector without needing
further modifications because only the first element is blended into ShiftedCarries while the rest are
not used. As a side note, it is also possible to blend the Carry into the last element of Carries before
rotating the carries. In that case, Carries (before the blend) should be returned as the Carry for the
next vector and the first carry would need to be (the less obvious) 0w−110(X−1)∗w.
This implements shl1or1′ with carry in 5 SIMD operations and thus is equally efficient as the variant
without carry.
9.2.2
Basic Subtraction
For subtraction, the carry starts as 0X∗w. This fact was exploited in the variant without carry to calculate
the first two carries within vector elements more efficiently. However, this is no longer the case with an
arbitrary carry, because the recursive carry formula Carryi = GTi−1 | (EQi−1 & Carryi−1) can no longer
be simplified. Hence, the loop has to be ran one more time. In addition, the carry for the next vector
also have to be calculated. This results in the following Basic Subtraction with carry:
procedure subbsc
′(A : vec, B : vec, Carry : vec) : vec × vec // A −B −Carry
GT ←B b> A
EQ ←A b= B
Carries ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Carry) // 0th carry.
Pos ←[0, . . . , 0, 0, 1]
for j ∈1 . . . X −1 do
Temp ←GT b| (EQ b& Carries)
Temp ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Temp)
Pos ←shuffle[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1](Pos)
Carries ←blend[Pos](Carries, Temp) // j’th carry.
NewCarry ←GT b| (EQ b& Carries) // X’th carry.
return (A b−B) b+ Carries, NewCarry
Listing 9.3: SIMD procedure to subtract two arbitrary vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers with carry using
Basic Subtraction that calculates the carries one by one.
As usual, the two operands are first compared. All vector elements in Carry are rotated one element
forward in order to move the last element to the first element, so the first (0th) carry is in the correct
element (this will make sense later). Next, the vector Pos that is responsible for inserting the correct
carry in the loop is initialized. Note that the first usage is rotated once compared to the initialized value.
The loop to calculate Carries are ran X −1 times which is one more than in the variant without Carry.
37
==PAGE:39==
The loop calculates the recursive formula Carryj = GTj−1 |(EQj−1 &Carryj−1) using bitwise operators,
rotating all vector elements in Temp and Pos one element forward and then insert the calculated carry
in Carries. After the loop, all carries for the current vector have been calculated. However, the carry
for the next vector needs to be calculated. After the loop, the last element in Carries is CarriesX−1.
Hence, CarryX−1 = GTX−1 | (EQX−1 & CarryX−1) can be calculated directly without any shuffles and
ends up as the last element in NewCarry. This is also why all vector elements in Carry are rotated one
element forward in the start. Hence, it only remains to return the actual result and the NewCarry.
Like the variant without carry, up to 5 SIMD operations are used per carry within the current vector.
In addition, 6 SIMD operations are used for comparisons, calculating the carry for the next vector and
calculate the actual result. There are X vector elements and thus it uses O(X) time.
9.2.3
Thorup Subtraction
The carry for Thorup Subtraction will be a bit different than the previous two, since the carry will be a
bit (integer that is 0 or 1) rather than a vector. This is rather clear when the carries are calculated in a
bitmask. The procedure is as follows:
procedure subthr
′(A : vec, B : vec, , carry : int) : vec × int // A −B −carry
GT ←B b> A
EQ ←A b= B
gt ←msbs(GT)
eq ←msbs(EQ)
packedCarries ←(((gt << 1) | carry) + eq) ⊕eq
nextCarry ←packedCarries >> X
Carries ←mask2vec(packedCarries)
return (A b−B) b+ Carries, nextCarry
Listing 9.4: SIMD procedure to subtract two arbitrary vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers with carry using
Thorup Subtraction that calculates the carries in one go using non-vector operations.
It first makes the usual comparisons and then extract the results in bitmasks. The carries are then
calculated as in section 8.2.3 with the exception that the carry is bitwise-or’ed with gt << 1. The carry
is inserted here since both carry and gt << 1 are the certain carries generated by the recursive formula.
Next, the carry for the next element is simply the X’th bit of packedCarries. Note that this requires
X < w as opposed to the variant without carry that only required X ≤w. In case X = w, one might
check an overflow flag from the addition or check if one of the operands is larger than the result. Finally,
the actual result is calculated and returned together with the carry for the next vector.
This uses 7 SIMD operations and 5 normal operations assuming X < w which is constant O(1) time.
9.2.4
Specialized Subtraction
The carries were relatively simple to add for the previous procedures because they calculated the actual
carries using the previous vector. However, Specialized Subtraction might need the carry generated by
any vector before the current vector. In case vector j needs the carry from vector i < j −1, then all
vectors within vector i + 1 and j −1 also need the carry from vector i because they contain the middle
38
==PAGE:40==
elements that pass on the carry to the next vector element as described in section 8.2.4. Hence, it is
possible to calculate the carries for the current vector by only using the current and previous vector if the
carry is passed on in the Carry. Another interesting observation is that if the carry has to be passed on,
then the result for that vector is going to be 0X∗w or 1X∗w because no bits can be set within a bit-pair
in which case the calculation is 0X∗w −0X∗w −carry where carry is 0 or 1. However, this observation
will not be used since it adds more operations for the opposite case (that likely is more common) and it
can only happen when there is a bounded gap x(aj, bj) with bj −aj ≥X ∗w such that I and F have no
set bits in a whole vector. Therefore, the procedure is as follows:
procedure subspz
′(A : vec, B : vec, S′ : vec, O : vec, po : int, Carry : vec) : vec × vec // A −B −Carry
GT ←B c
>> O
GTWithCarry ←blend[1, 0, 0, . . . , 0](GT, Carry)
Carries ←shuffle[S′](GTWithCarry)
return (A b−B) b−Carries, (po ? Carries : GT)
Listing 9.5: SIMD procedure to subtract two vectors as (X ∗w)-bit integers with carry using Specialized
Subtraction where the carry generating bits and carry destinations are known beforehand.
The first thing to note is that there are two new arguments, namely the Carry and a boolean flag
po (pass on). As usual, the Carry starts as 0X∗w. The last element in Carry will contain the actual
carry that will be used, because the carry generated by the last element is not used in the current vector
but only in the next vector. This allows to blend in the Carry without having to shuffle the elements.
po is true when the carry given to the last element should be passed on as the carry to the next vector.
Note that this is not only the case described above, but also covers the case (in vector i) when the carry
generated to the last element should be passed to the next vector. The shuffle indexes in S also need to
be changed since they are currently global positions (the index for the previous element) rather than a
local index in the current vector. If a global position in S points to the same vector then set it to the
global position modulo X to get the local index. Otherwise, set the value to X −1 to pick the carry from
the previous vector that is blended in at the last ((X −1)’th) element. The modified vector containing
the local shuffle indexes will be denoted S′.
The procedure first calculates the carries generated in this vector as GT like in the variant without
carry. The Carry is then inserted in GT at the last element since a carry generated by the last element
will not be used in the current vector. Next, the carries are moved to the desired elements. Finally, the
actual subtraction is calculated and the correct carry is returned based on the po flag. When the po flag
is false, then only the last element may generate a carry for the next vector and thus GT can be used as
Carry. If po is true, then either an element (except the last element) in the current vector may generate
a carry that should be passed on to the next vector (the extra case) or the previous vector gave a carry
that should be passed on. In both cases, the last element received the generated carry and thus Carries
can be used as the carry for the next vector.
The description above is likely very confusing. Therefore, an example with four vectors of X = 2
elements each of w = 4 bits will be used to (hopefully) make this more clear:
