Starting from the right most-column (the first vector), the first (0th) element gets its carry from the last
(1st) element in Carry since it is the position where the carry from the previous vector is blended in.
The last element gets its carry from the first element since the blue bit-pair starts in the first element.
The green carry is generated in the last element and should be used in the following vectors. Therefore,
po should be false to pick GT as the carry to correctly get the green carry.
In the second vector, both elements get their carries from the last element that contains the carry
from the previous vector. The green bit-pair does not end in this vector, so the carry in the last element
in Carries should be passed on to the next vector and thus po should be true. Note that Carry could
in this case also have been passed on, but the next vector shows why Carries is the best choice.
In the third vector, the first element again gets the carry from the previous vector. The first element
also generates the red carry that is needed by the last element. The red bit-pair does not end here, so
the red carry needs to be passed on to the next vector. However, GT does not have it in the last element
so a shuffle would be needed. On the other hand, Carries has the red carry in the last element and thus
can be used by having po set to true.
In the fourth vector, both elements get the carries from the last element that has the red carry from
the previous vector.
This example clear shows why po should be true to pick Carries when a carry generated by the
previous vector or by the current vector – except in the last element. The second and third vectors
show why Carries needs to be picked as the carry for next vector to have the correct carry at the last
element. The first vector shows why the a carry generated in last element needs to pick GT, because
GTWithCarry throws it away such that it cannot occur in Carries.
40
==PAGE:42==
Specialized Subtraction with carry uses 5 SIMD operations and 1 normal operation (the ternary
operator). This is two operations more than the variant without carry but it still uses constant time.
As a final remark, the changes needed for calculating the updated S′ variable and how the po flags are
determined in the preprocessing are shown in Listing 9.6. The arrays of vectors are initialized with vector
operations that modifies a whole vector of X element at a time while the preprocessing of the pattern
will modify single vector elements as they were elements in an array of words. To avoid double indexing
for accessing single words, single brackets [k] accesses the k’th word when viewing the array as an array
of words while double brackets [[k]] accesses the k’th vector when viewing it as an array of vectors.
/* Preprocessing */
for k ∈0 . . . ⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉−1 do
I[[k]] ←0X∗w, F[[k]] ←0X∗w, for c ∈Σ do B[c][[k]] ←0X∗w
G[[k]] ←0X∗w, N[[k]] ←0X∗w
S′[[k]] ←[X −2, X −3, . . . , 1, 0, X −1], O[[k]] ←broadcast(w), po[k] ←0
M ←0X∗w
i ←0
start ←P0 is x(a0, b0) ? a0 : 0
for j ∈(P0 is x(a0, b0) ? 1 : 0) . . . m −1 do
if Pj is x(aj, bj) then
initialIndex ←index(i −1)
finalIndex ←index(i + bj −aj)
I[initialIndex] ←setBit(I[initialIndex], offset(i −1))
if i + bj −aj < L then
F[finalIndex] ←setBit(F[finalIndex], offset(i + bj −aj))
else // i + bj −aj = L (out of bounds).
finalIndex ←finalIndex −1
setBits′(G, i, bj)
if initialIndex < finalIndex then
O[initialIndex] ←offset(i −1)
localIndex ←initialIndex & (X −1)
lastElement ←min(initialIndex | (X −1), finalIndex)
for k ∈initialIndex + 1 . . . lastElement do S′[k] ←localIndex
for k ∈lastElement + 1 . . . finalIndex do S′[k] ←X −1
for k ∈⌊(localIndex + 1)/X⌋. . . ⌊finalIndex/X⌋do po[k] ←1
// No changes for Pj is [Cj](nj) and Pj is {Cj}(nj).
for k ∈0 . . . ⌈L/(X ∗w)⌉−1, c ∈Σ do B[c][[k]] ←(B[c][[k]] b⊕N[[k]])b| G[[k]]
M[index(i −1) % X] ←setBit(0w, offset(i −1))
Listing 9.6: Preprocessing of the algorithm that uses multiple vectors as bitmasks. Changes compared
to Listing 8.6 are highlighted in red.
41
==PAGE:43==
10
Parallel Simulation with SIMD
Another way to utilize SIMD is to perform the simulation in parallel. One option is to match one text
with X patterns of max lengths that fits within a computer word (L ≤w). Another option is to match
X texts with one pattern with L ≤w. Both these options requires that you have more than one pattern
or text to match in. In addition, they are more or less straight forward to make. Therefore, this section
will look at how to make a parallel implementation with just one pattern and one text.
10.1
Splitting into Parallel Tasks
The first thing to note is that the algorithm is one long loop carried dependency chain where each
iteration depends on the previous, so it is not really suited to be split into multiple jobs that run in
parallel. Therefore, the best option is to split the text T into X slices. However, T cannot just be split
into X disjoint slices of equal size. For example, if P = A−x(2, 4)−A, T = ACCACCACCAACCCCA
and X = 4, then a disjoint equal split would give ACCA, CCAC, CAAC and CCCA. Running the
algorithm on each slice would only give one match while there are five matches in total. It is the matches
across the different slices that are missing. A match could potentially start at the last character in a slice
and have a length of L. Therefore, an overlap of L −1 characters is needed. This effectively means each
slice is extended with L −1 characters at the end. Hence, that the last L −1 of T can be excluded from
the distributed characters since these will be append to the last slice.
Patterns that start with a bounded gap x(a0, b0) also needs to be taken into account because the
first a0 characters are skipped. The variable start ←P0 is x(a0, b0) ? a0 : 0 tells how many characters
that are skipped. Note that this also makes the max length of the pattern start = a0 shorter. Hence,
there are n −start −(L −start −1) = n −L + 1 characters that should be split into X slices. In case
n−L+1 is negative, it cannot payoff to split and the single word algorithm is used. Each slice then has a
length of ⌊(n −L + 1)/X⌋plus the L −start −1 extra characters. This length of a slice is rounded down
to avoid going out of bounds. In case there are characters left, these are added to the last slice where
the extra characters are handled using the single word algorithm. Returning to the example, there are
16 −(6 −1) = 11 characters to distribute which gives slices with a length of ⌊11/4⌋+ (6 −0 −1) = 2 + 5
characters and thus the slices becomes:
Part 1 : A C
|{z}
Main
C A C C A
|
{z
}
Extra
(matches at 3, 6)
Part 2 :
C A
|{z}
Main
C C A C C
|
{z
}
Extra
(matches at 6)
Part 3 :
C C
|{z}
Main
A C C A A
|
{z
}
Extra
(matches at 9, 10)
Part 4 :
A C
|{z}
Main
C A A C C
|
{z
}
Extra
C C A
| {z }
Leftovers
(matches at 9, 10, 15)
An interesting observation is that first and second slice both contains a match at 6 while the last two
slices both contain matches at 9 and 10. Thus, duplicates also have to be dealt with. Luckily, this turns
out to be very simple to handle.
Consider the L −start −1 overlapping characters within slice i and slice i + 1. Any match found in
slice i + 1 within the first L −start −1 characters will also be found in slice i, since they contain the
same substring. However, slice i might find more matches within the overlapping L−start−1 characters
because it has characters before the last L −start −1 characters that might generate more matches.
42
==PAGE:44==
Hence, you can simply discard any match within the first L −start −1 characters (inclusive) of all slices,
except the first slice, to remove all duplicates. Furthermore, the matches can be reported in ascending
order by collecting the matches from each slice (they are sorted) and then concatenate all the matches
into one sequence.
The overhead in terms of rescanned characters is (X −1)(L −start −1). On the other hand, X
characters are handled per iteration, so the total number of iterations is ⌈(n+(X−1)(L−start−1))/X⌉=
O(n/X + L).
10.2
Creating Tasks and Collecting Results
Now, it has been decided how to split the problem into parallel tasks and found how to report the
matches in the right order without duplicates. The simulation is performed using the SIMD-version of
each operation in the single word version. However, it remains how to generate a vector with the tasks
and how to extract the results from a vector. The entire algorithm is shown below in Listing 10.1 and
it will afterwards be explained how it works. The red parts shows how tasks are created and how the
results are collected. Variables with an arrow on top shows the vector version of the single word variable,
for instance ⃗I is a vector that contains the value of I in all elements.
43
==PAGE:45==
/* Preprocessing */
[...] // Single word preprocessing like in Listing 5.2.
⃗I ←broadcast(I), ⃗F ←broadcast(F), ⃗M ←broadcast(M)
totalLength ←n −L + 1
distance ←⌊totalLength/X⌋
overlap ←L −start −1
sliceLength ←distance + overlap
for j ∈0 . . . X −1 do results[j] ←empty
/* Parallel Matching */
⃗D ←0X∗w
if distance > 0 then
for j ∈start . . . start + sliceLength −1 do
c ⃗B ←[B[Tj+(X−1)∗distance], . . . , B[Tj+distance], B[Tj]]
⃗D ←(( ⃗D c
<< 1)b| (0w−11)X) b& c ⃗B
c⃗I ←⃗D b& ⃗I, c⃗F ←⃗F b& b∼c⃗I
⃗D ←⃗Db| ((c⃗F b−c⃗I) b& b∼⃗F)
matches ←msbs(( ⃗D b& ⃗M) b̸= 0X∗w)
if j < start + overlap then
if matches & 0X−11 ̸= 0 then add match in results[0] ending at j
else
while matches ̸= 0 do
i ←trailing0s(matches)
add match in results[i] ending at j + i ∗distance
matches ←matches & (matches −1)
/* Sequential Matching */
D ←⃗DX−1
for j ∈start + sliceLength + (X −1) ∗distance . . . n −1 do
D ←((D c
<< 1) | 0w−11) & B[Tj]
cI ←D & I, cF ←F & ∼cI
D ←D | ((cF −cI) & ∼F)
if (D & M) ̸= 0L then add match in results[X −1] ending at j
/* Reporting */
for i ∈0 . . . X −1 do report all matches in results[i]
Listing 10.1: Parallelized version that uses SIMD to simulate the automaton in parallel. The red parts
shows how tasks are created and how the results are collected.
44
==PAGE:46==
The preprocessing for the single word-version is used.
After that, the bitmasks I, F and M are
broadcasted into vectors, the new variables/values mentioned in section 10.1 are calculated and containers
(results) are created to collect results for each parallel simulation. The parallel matching only runs if
the text T can be split into tasks with positive length (distance > 0). In the parallel matching, the
vector c ⃗B of tasks is created by looking up the current character in each slice in the table B. Next,
the transitions are simulated similar to the single word-version using the vector operators. Finally, each
slice is checked for matches (( ⃗D b& ⃗M) b̸= 0X∗w) and the results are collected in a bitmask called matches
using msbs. If the algorithm is within the overlapping (first overlap = L −start −1) characters, then
only matches from the first part is collected as explained in section 10.1. In this case, it is checked if the
first bit, that represents a match in the first slice, is set and if that is the case then a match is added
to result[0] (the match is not reported yet). Otherwise, all slices may report matches. In this case, the
bitmask might have multiple set bits where the positions of the set bits are needed to report the correct
positions. One way is to keep a counter and shift the bits down one-by-one to the least significant bit,
but it uses linear time. Therefore, one may use binary search to speed up the process. A third option
is to count the number of trailing zeros since it gives the position i of the least significant set bit. The
position of the match is then j + i ∗distance. The least significant set bit can be cleared by bitwise-and
with matches −1. It flips all 0-bits up to and including the least 1-bit (also flipped) meaning that all
those bits are different in matches and matches−1. Hence, if the bits are different then one of them most
be 0 such that matches & (matches −1) clears all bits up to the least set bit in matches. Thus, matches
can be reported in this way while matches ̸= 0. When the parallel simulation is over, the simulation
continues on the last slice with the single word version. Finally, all matches are reported in order.
The preprocessing time is O(|P| + |Σ| + X) since only a constant number of constant time operations
and a loop creating X entries in results were added compared to the single word-version. As earlier
mentioned, only O(n/X + L) iterations are performed (ignoring the leftover characters). However, there
are X individual table lookups in each iteration which effectively negates any improvement in the time
complexity. There is not a more efficient way to perform the table lookups in memory than doing them
one-by-one, so it is clearly the bottleneck. In addition, there might also be up to n matches, but it can
of course be expressed it in terms of the number of occurrences (occ ≤n) instead of n. This means that
the time complexity is O(X(n/X + L) + occ) = O(n + XL). Even though the time complexity is worse
than previous versions, there are fewer iterations and thus fewer operations in total since the complexity
only reflects the creation of the task vector and the case where occ = O(n).
10.3
Multiple Words
In case the max length of a pattern does not fit within a w-bit word (L ≤w), then multiple words has
to be used to store each bitmask. In that case it is still possible to use the above approach but one
need to combine it with the approach presented in section 6. In that case the preprocessing will take
O((|P| + |Σ|)⌈L/w⌉+ X) time as it then uses the multiple words preprocessing. The matching will take
O((n+XL)⌈L/w⌉) time because the creation of tasks and the simulation will have to be repeated ⌈L/w⌉
times per character in T. The collection of results still only need to be performed for the last word like
in section 6 but this does not improve the time complexity.
45
==PAGE:47==
11
Implementation Overview
In this thesis, implementations of the different version on a specific CPU will be considered. The CPU is
an Intel Core i7-1195G7 (11th generation) processor with codename Tiger Lake (Willow Cove microar-
chitecture), 12 MB cache and up to 5.00 GHz (likely only up to 2.90 GHz) [11]. This processor supports
the x86-64 instruction set that allows operating with 64-bit integers. In addition, it also supports the
instruction set extensions AVX2 and AVX-512 that allows to operate on multiple registers in parallel
(SIMD). AVX stands for Advanced Vector Extensions where AVX2 is the second version and AVX-512 is
the third version [13]. The implementation is in the programming language C with Intel Intrinsics and
compiled with GCC 12.2 where the optimization flag -O1 is used in order to inline small procedures into
each version to keep the code cleaner and avoid the overhead of inlining.
The following sections will present and analyze implementations using the x86-64, AVX2 and AVX512
instruction sets. They will be based on the theoretical algorithms presented so far. Table 11.1 shows an
overview of all versions. match00 is based on the naive version presented in section 2.5 while the remaining
versions are based on the different variants of the main algorithm. All of these (except match00) will
end on either a, b, c or d. Versions ending with a are only using one word or vector meaning that they
only support a limited maximum length (L). Versions ending with b use multiple words or vectors to
allow any maximum length. Versions ending with c are assembly optimized versions of the corresponding
version ending with a and are presented later in section 16. The versions ending with d builds on top of
the versions ending on c and stores the lookup table in AVX-registers instead of memory. Details can be
found in section 16.3. The Ref-column refers to the section which the version is based on. All versions
handle the edge cases described in section 5. Note that match01b is an unoptimized version of match02b.
In particular, it sets individual bits in the preprocessing similar to match01a and does not implement
any optimizations mentioned in section 4 and section 6. For each of the parallel versions (match09x and
match10x), there is a variant using 8-bit, 16-bit, 32-bit and 64-bit elements.
11.1
Latency Chain Analysis
Today, most modern processors like Tiger Lake are so called out-of-order processors that may swap
the order of independent instructions to execute multiple instructions per clock cycle to better utilize
the throughput of instructions. This makes it very difficult to analyze the theoretical performance of
the different theoretical algorithms presented so far. However, the out-of-order processors cannot move
instructions before instructions it depends on or after instructions that depends on it. It is possible to
find the longest dependency chain without knowledge about the processor by inspecting the theoretical
algorithms. However, this does not tell which of the algorithms that are most efficient because different
instructions may have different latencies. For instance, there could be one long dependency chain of ten
additions and another with five multiplications. The multiplication chain is shortest but is slower if the
additions have a latency of 1 clock cycle (a latency of 1 ∗10 = 10 clock cycles) while multiplications have
a latency of 3 clock cycles (a latency of 3 ∗5 = 15 clock cycles). Hence, it is not enough to analyze the
dependency chain to estimate the performance. Similarly, just counting the number of operations may
neither be accurate since a longer instruction sequence might better unitize the throughput of a processor.
For instance, there could be 10 independent additions and 5 independent multiplication. The 10 additions
may be executed in just 3 clock cycles if the processor can execute 4 additions per clock cycle. This is
faster than the multiplications if the processor only can execute 1 multiplication per clock cycle. At this
point, it is already quite complex to analyze the actual performance. However, some modern processor
like Tiger Lake also splits complex instructions into so called micro-operations (uops/µops). For instance,
an instruction that increases the value of a variable in memory may be split into three µops: one for

of supported patterns and the asymptotic time complexities are shown.
read, one for modification and one for write (usually, the store operation is a separate instruction). The
complexity does not stop there, since these processors contains a limited number of execution units that
only can execute a subset of the possible µops. µops are distributed to the execution units via execution
ports that adds an additional bottleneck. In addition, µops may sometimes be fused for faster execution.
For further details, please consult [17] (page 1-4).
47
==PAGE:49==
It is possible to make the complex analysis like Agner does in [25] (section 12.7), but a far simpler
analysis model will be used in the following sections. Define the latency chain as the total latency when
executing the dependency chain that results in the largest latency.
It is assumed that the processor
can execute arbitrarily many instructions in parallel as long as the data needed for the instructions are
ready. This is a very simplified model for analyzing the actual performance since this does not take the
throughput and execution units/ports into account. It is not going to show the whole truth but as will
be seen, it is going to give a good estimate for the performance because most of the operations depend
on the result of the previous operation. For simplicity, memory access will be ignored by assuming that
any memory access always give a hit in the fastest cache and that any value from memory is preloaded
into a register such that memory access has a latency of 0. In addition, it is also assumed that there
are enough registers to hold all variables and constants at all time – or that constants can be supplied
directly in the instruction opcode. The analysis will also disregard the time needed to report a match
since all versions report the same matches in the same way except that the parallel versions that use a
few extra operations for reporting but this will be ignored. Only the matching phase will be analyzed
because it will likely be the bottleneck in real world applications where the text T is much longer than
the pattern P.
48
==PAGE:50==
12
Implementation in x86-64
In this section, the latency and throughput of relevant instructions in the x86-64 instruction set will
be presented before the matching phase of match01x and match02x will be analyzed by analyzing their
latency chains.
12.1
Latency and Throughput Data
Table 12.1 shows the latency and throughput of selected range of instructions in the instruction set x86-64
for the Intel Tiger Lake CPU. All values are the minimum value that disregards cache misses and other
slow downs. The Operation-column shows the operation similar to the notation used so far while the
Instruction-column tells the name of the instruction. The Operand Size-column refers to the number
of bits used in the 64-bit registers for where the latency and throughput applies. Latency is measured
in clock cycles while the throughput is written as the reciprocal throughput [17].
Throughput is the
number of the same instruction (in fact µops) that can be executed within the same clock cycle assuming
the instructions are independent. The reciprocal throughput is then the average number of clock cycles
assuming maximum throughput. A throughput of 4 gives a reciprocal throughput of 1/4 = 0.25. The
Notes-column contains additional notes, constraints and eventually the values from sources that disagrees
with the chosen values (L for latency and T for throughput).
The latency and throughput data in Table 12.1 is from a mix of different sources since most of the
sources were either incomplete, for another CPU or disagreed with the others. Besides that, the sources
were found in different points in time. First, the official documentation from Intel [14][15][16] was used but
it only contained data for other CPUs, for instance for the Ice Lake (10th generation) microarchitecture.
It contains the theorical latencies and throughputs. Note that the documentation for Ice Lake [15] stated

that the data for Intel Xeon Scalable Processors [16] with Skylake Server microarchitecture can be used

and for different CPUs while the test methods might be inaccurate or only provide upper bounds. There-

the data for similarly operations by taking the operand size (element size for vector instructions) into
account. The sources are shown as superscripted letters after the values where:

12.2

The matching loop of match01a is the one presented in section 2.7 without any optimizations but with
the fixes for the edge cases presented in section 5. The implementation is shown in Table 12.2. The
second column contains the actual code. The O-column shows the operation used while the I -column
show the instruction name. The L-column shows the latency of the chosen instruction. The D-column
shows the dependencies to previous instructions/inputs that are uniquely identified by the first column
(#). Dependencies to variables that are constant after the preprocessing, for instance B, I, M and i, are
not noted in the D-column. The S-column shows when the instruction at the earliest can start and the
F-column shows when the result is ready (finished). As seen in Table 12.2, all instructions depend on
the previous instruction which gives a latency chain of 11 clock cycles. Note that checking a branching
condition and doing the jump will for simplicity be modeled as executing cmp.

12.3
Latency Chain for match02a
The main difference between match01a and match02a is the optimizations in the preprocessing presented
in section 4. However, match02a also contains a few implementation optimizations of the matching loop
compared to match01a above.
The first observation is that D << 1 = 2 ∗D = D + D meaning that the left bit shift can be replaced
with an addition. It has the same latency (1) as bit shift but a better throughput (0.25 < 0.5) so it
eventually could be executed at the same time as another addition. Furthermore, D << 1 shifts in one 0
as the least significant bit (LSB) such that (D << 1) & 0w−11 = 0w. This implies that (D << 1) | 0w−11 =
(D << 1) + 0w−11 because the addition will not generate any carries. Hence, (D << 1) | 0w−11 = D ∗2 + 1
which allows usage of the lea (load efficient address) instruction to reduce the latency by 1 for the two
operations in line 1 and 2! The next observation is that the calculations of ncI and nF are superfluous if
the so-called andn ((∼a) & b) instruction is used. This only reduces the latency chain by 1 by eliminating
line 5 in Table 12.2 because ∼F could be calculated before the loop like nF. However, using andn requires
one register less which might reduce the number of mov instructions needed in case of limited registers
(ignored here). Hence, the latency chain is only 9 clock cycles as seen in Table 12.3.

12.4
Latency Chain for match01b
The multiple words version match01b contains an inner loop in the matching loop which will be analyzed
first. Like match01a, the optimizations mentioned in section 6.2 are left out to keep it as close as possible
to the original algorithm by Navarro and Raffinot [5]. The analysis is presented in Table 12.4 where an
additional variable isMatch is introduced. It keeps track of the current result of D & M ̸= 0L across
words. This is in fact calculated as D & M = M because all except the last word will be 0w since M only
contains one 1-bit. This is also why the alternative calculation is possible.
As opposed to match01a, it is not just one long dependency chain but much more work can potentially
run in parallel because the carries also need to be calculated. The incrementation of k is also included
because it is used on line 17 which means that it cannot be changed before that line is finished. The
longest latency chain has a latency of 13 but this is to update isMatch which is first needed at the very
end after 12 clock cycles. This means that there is plenty of time to wait for this in the next iteration
meaning that the effective latency chain is 12 because k is needed at the very start of the next iteration.
The only exception is after the final iteration where isMatch is needed directly after the loop.
Now, it only remains to analyze the matching loop as shown in Table 12.5. The latency of the inner
loop is set to t = 12⌈L/w⌉+ 1 because it performs ⌈L/w⌉iterations where all except the last iteration
51
==PAGE:53==
have a latency chain of 12. The last iteration has a latency chain of 13 as discussed above which gives
the +1. Hence, the latency chain is 12⌈L/w⌉+ 3 clock cycles.

12.5
Latency Chain for match02b
Table 12.6 contains the analysis of the inner matching loop of match02b.
It contains the same two
optimizations as for match02a, namely the usage of the lea instruction (line 2) and using andn, to save
2 clock cycles in total. It also avoids the comparison cIc < cI as well as the part for calculating isMatch
as described in section 6.2. This brings the latency chain down to 8 clock cycles.
The matching loop in Table 12.7 then has a latency chain of 8⌈L/w⌉+3 clock cycles because the inner
loop has a latency of t = 8⌈L/w⌉. Note that ⌈L/w⌉−start −1 = index(i −1) accesses the last word and
is computed before the loop. Furthermore, recall that M is a single word as described in section 6.2.

13
Implementation in Advanced Vector Extension 2 (AVX2)
Most processors that support SIMD instructions do it in the form of Advanced Vector Extensions (AVX).
In this section, AVX2 that operates on 256 bits will considered [13]. There are 16 vector registers in
AVX2 and it allows to operate on the bits in vectors of 64-bit, 32-bit, 16-bit and 8-bit elements. Most of
the time, four 64-bit elements will be used to have as few elements as possible. However, AVX2 does not
directly implement all the functionality needed in section 7. It requires usage of some additional tricks
and usage of different element sizes to overcome this limitation. Furthermore, some instructions might be
more expensive than an alternative that achieves the same as already seen with the lea instruction. In
the end of this section, match03x, match04x and match05x are implemented in AVX2 by implementing
each of the procedures shown in section 8 and section 9 before the matching loop is considered.
13.1
Limitations
The following sections will outline the limitations in AVX2 and how to handle them as efficient as possible.
13.1.1
Unsigned Addition and Subtraction
Addition and subtraction is only supported for signed integers. However, signed and unsigned two’s
complement addition/subtraction work in the same way, so that is not a problem.
13.1.2
Bitwise-not
Bitwise-not b∼V is not supported directly but can be implemented by using bitwise-xor with 1256 meaning
b∼V ≡V b⊕1256. It is clear to see this from a truth table.
13.1.3
Unsigned Comparison
AVX2 only supports equality (A b= B) and greater than (A b> B) for signed integers. For this reason,
a subscripted s or u will from now on be used whenever a comparison operator is used to distinguish
the signed version from the unsigned version unless signedness does not matter. Signed equality can be
used as unsigned equality since both equalities check if all bits are the same. Not equal can then be
implemented by negating the result using bitwise-xor with 1256 as seen above. However, unsigned greater
than is more cumbersome when only signed greater than is available.
In order to compare two integers as unsigned with signed comparison, one can add 10w−1 to the
two integers before the comparison [19].
It only flips the sign bit (MSB) in both integers such that
negative integers becomes non-negative and visa versa. Hence, bitwise-xor with 10w−1 can also be used
[19]. However, it is not obvious that it works, so all four cases for a >u b will be considered. Note
that the only difference between signed and unsigned comparison [20] is that signed comparison has
special cases when the sign bits are different. Hence, if msb(a) = msb(b) then the sign bits are the
same before and after they are flipped. Thus, signed comparison gives the same result as unsigned. If
msb(a) = 0 while msb(b) = 1 then a >u b is false. Flipping the sign bits gives msb(a + 10w−1) = 1 while
msb(b + 10w−1) = 0 and thus a + 10w−1 >s b + 10w−1 is false since a is negative while b is non-negative.
Finally, having msb(a) = 1 while msb(b) = 0 means that a >u b holds. Flipping the sign bits gives
msb(a + 10w−1) = 0 while msb(b +10w−1) = 1 and thus a +10w−1 >s b +10w−1 is true. Hence, unsigned
greater than can be achieved using signed comparison and addition (or bitwise-xor) with 10w−1. The
remaining unsigned comparison operators can be implemented using unsigned greater than and then
eventually flip the operands and/or negate the result.
54
==PAGE:56==
13.1.4
The broadcast Function
AVX2 does not support to broadcast a register or immediate value directly to a vector. broadcast needs
to load the value from memory which adds an extra overhead. Hence, register values need to be stored
(using mov) before broadcasting. Interestingly enough, the GCC compiler prefers this pattern over other
methods of broadcasting that appears to have lower latency.
The Intel Optimization Reference Manual [23] (section 3.6.4) mentions that the processor in question
supports store-forwarding which in this case means that broadcast does not have to wait for the store
(mov) instruction to finish but can load the data directly.
It is a bit unclear what the actual effect
is, but according to Agner Fog [26] (section 12.13), the Tiger Lake processor supports (undocumented)
fast-forwarding that yields 0 latency. However, it is a bit unclear if this also eliminates the latency of
the loading part of the broadcast. It will be assumed that it eliminates the loading part because it will
otherwise be slower than the alternatives and it allows analyzing the latency chain without considering
memory. Note that Agner [26] (section 12.13) mentions that he has observed that it does not work for
broadcasting a 16-bit word, but this will be ignored.
13.1.5
The shuffle Function
It should be noted that AVX2 only fully supports shuffle for 32-bit elements when the indexes are given
as a (variable) vector. AVX2 supports shuffle with a constant for 64-bit elements. There exists other
versions that accepts constants or vectors but they only allow shuffling elements within the first or/last
128 bits or swapping the first and last 128 bits. Hence, it might be possible to implement shuffle for 64-bit
elements using a combination of the limited shuffle functions and blend, but it will be very slow compared
to using 32-bit elements. Therefore, 32-bit elements will be used for the Specialized Subtraction.
AVX2 supports byte (not bit!)
shifts on two 128-bit elements meaning that one can move every
element forward at the cost of zeroing out some elements because bits are shifted out while shifting in
zeros. For instance, shifting 8 bytes forward on the vector [4, 3, 2, 1] (64-bit elements), the result will be
[3, 0, 1, 0]. Shifting 4 bytes forward in a 32-bit vector [8, 7, 6, 5, 4, 3, 2, 1], the result is [7, 6, 5, 0, 3, 2, 1, 0]
since only 32 bits per 128 bits are lost. Hence, this can be used as a special kind of shuffle that will be
denoted as shuffle[2, z, 0, z] for 64-bit elements and shuffle[6, 5, 4, z, 2, 1, 0, z] for 32-bit elements. The z
represents that the element is zeroed out.
13.1.6
The mask2vec Function
AVX2 does not support the mask2vec function needed for Thorup Subtraction. It can be implemented
using the following combination of instructions:
procedure mask2vec(mask : int) : vec
Bits ←[1 << (X −1), 1 << (X −2), . . . , 1 << 0]
V ←broadcast(mask)
W ←V b& Bits
Neg ←W b= 0X∗w
return Neg b⊕0X∗w
Listing 13.1: Procedure to perform the unsupported mask2vec function in AVX2.
55
==PAGE:57==
Recall that the goal is to generate a vector where the i’th element is 1w if the i’th bit in the input
bitmask is set. The idea is to have a vector Bits where the i’th element has the i’th bit set. These can
be used to check if the i’th bit is set in the mask. The mask can then be broadcasted to all elements in
a vector followed by bitwise-and with Bits. This leaves a 1-bit in the i’th element if the i’th bit in mask
was set and otherwise leaves 0w. To get 1w in elements with a 1-bit, it is checked if they are not equal
to 0w (similar to the trick with the underflow bits in section 8.2.4). As explained above, not equal is two
operations.

Table 13.1: Latency and throughput for selected AVX2 instructions for Tiger Lake. y is an AVX2 vector
in a vector register while i is an immediate value. Labeling: (v) vector control argument, (c) constant

56
==PAGE:58==
Table 13.1 shows the latency and throughput of selected AVX2 instructions in a similar format as in
Table 12.1. However, note that the Operand Size-column has been replaced with Element Size for the
size of each of the elements in a vector. Further, note that the instructions are now specified as regular
expressions to cover that there are different instructions for different element sizes. There are the following
suffixes [13] (section 4): b stands for byte (8 bits), w stands for word (16 bits), d stands for doubleword
(32 bits), q stands for quadword (64 bits) and dq stands for double quadword (128 bits). However, note
that vmovdqa moves 256 bits. In addition, only the floating point version exists for some instructions.
In those, ps stands for packed single precision (floats, 32 bits) and pd stands for packed double precision
(doubles, 64 bits).
The table includes latency and throughput for the custom procedures presented in section 13.1. For
those procedures, the listed latency is the total latency of the longest latency chain. The (reciprocal)
throughput of the custom procedures are stated as the lowest value to list the best possible throughput.
The best possible throughput is the sum of the individual throughputs which can be achieved by inter-
leaving multiple of the same operation on independent data. For an out-of-order processor, the chosen
throughput calculation for the custom procedures gives throughputs similar to those listed in the sources
since they assume the operations does not depend on each other. Also note that the data for vptest and
vmovdqa are different in most of the sources. Furthermore, it is assumed that it is free to copy a vector
using vmovdqa due to register allocation and renaming as mentioned in [26] (section 12.8).
13.3
Latency Chain of shl1or1
The common procedure shl1or1 from section 8.2.1 is analyzed as the first in Table 13.2. Note that the
argument types are omitted to keep the table (and future tables) more compact. Arguments starting
with an uppercase letter is a vector (type vec) in a vector register while the others are integers (type int)
in either a normal register or in a mask register (in AVX-512).
The version that includes the bitwise-or with 0X∗w1 (instead of doing it afterwards) is used because
it uses one operation less in total. All expressions containing w = 64 and X = 4 have been evaluated.
Like for integers, it also holds for vectors that V c
<< 1 = V b+ V . Therefore, addition will be used because
it has better throughput. This gives a latency chain of 6 clock cycles.

13.4
Latency Chain of Basic Subtraction (match03a)
Compared to the version of Basic Subtraction in section 8.2.2, a lot of things have been optimized. First
of all, the calculation of the 0th (always zero) and 1st carry is performed in one operation by exploiting
the AVX2 byte shift shuffle[2, z, 0, z]. It shifts GT0 to Carries1 while shifting in 0w at Carries0 and thus
is a huge improvement. The loop has been unrolled to avoid the overhead of a loop and to enable usage
of the constant version of blend for 64-bit elements since the constant needs to be known at compile
57
==PAGE:59==
time. Furthermore, it allows using byte shift every second iteration with better latency and throughput
than shuffle with a constant. As usual note that the carries are added since they are 1w = −1. This
gives a Basic Subtraction with a latency chain of 15 clock cycles which less than 4 cycles per element
and thus considerably better than expected. Also note that the argument to blend is given as the binary
representation of an integer.

13.5
Latency Chain of Thorup Subtraction (match04a)
The implementation of Thorup Subtraction closely follows the version presented in section 8.2.3 using
the custom functions in AVX2. However, it is again possible to write (gt << 1) + eq = gt ∗2 + eq meaning
that the lea instruction can be used to reduce the latency by 1. Note that msbs extracts 4 bits which
are (implicitly) zero-extend to 64 bits in Table 13.4. It is worth noticing that the total latency is 15
clock cycles and thus is the same as Basic Subtraction. The main penalty is the lack of direct support
for mask2vec in AVX2. In addition, it should be noted that mask2vec makes use of broadcast that might
have longer latency than assumed as noted in section 13.1.4. Thus, Thorup Subtraction might have a
longer latency than 15 clock cycles.

Note that using 32-bit elements, the latency chain becomes 13 clock cycles.
58
==PAGE:60==
The observant reader will have noticed that 64-bit elements have been used in Table 13.4. However,
unsigned greater is (surprisingly) twice as fast for 32-bit elements (latency of 2) compared to 64-bit
elements (latency of 4). On the other hand, 32-bit elements give twice as many elements and thus twice
as many carries. All carries are calculated at once so this is actually not a problem and thus it is possible
to shave off 2 clock cycles to get down to a latency chain of 13 clock cycles.
13.6
Latency Chain of Specialized Subtraction (match05a)
Before presenting the implementation of Specialized Subtraction from section 8.2.4, it first has to be
determined which of the three methods that should be used to calculate GT. As noted in section 13.1.5,
32-bit elements has to be used since it is the only way to use shuffle with a vector in AVX2. The element
size does not matter since the subtraction does not depend on the number of vector elements and the
vector represents a 256-bit integer before and after the procedure. The only downside of using 32-bit
elements is that the preprocessing also needs to consider the 32-bit indexes to calculate S and O (or U)
which takes a bit extra time.
The first option is to use unsigned greater than cI b>u cF which has a latency of 2 for 32-bit elements.
The second option is to use (cF b&U) b̸=0256 which has a dependency chain of 3 (recall that not equal is two
operations) with a latency chain of 3 for both 32-bit and 64-bit elements. Hence, if 64-bit elements were
used, this would actually be faster than the unsigned comparison. However, both these are outperformed
by cI c
>> O that has a latency of just 1 for both 32-bit and 64-bit elements! Thus, there is a clear winner
that is used in the implementation in Table 13.5. Note that it generates 0w or 0w−11 as carries such
that the carries should be subtracted in contrast to the two subtraction procedures above. Specialized
Subtraction has a latency of just 5 clock cycles and thus should be (almost) 3 times faster than the
alternatives above!

13.7
Latency Chain of match03a, match04a and match05a
The only thing left in the analysis is to analyze the matching loop which is shown in Table 13.6. It uses
the vector version of andn to reduce the latency by 1 like in section 12.3. It also checks the bitwise-and
between D and M as vectors followed by msbs to avoid having to extract an element which is more
expensive (requires instructions not covered in Table 13.1). The vectorized way of checking for a match
is msb((D b& M) b̸= 0X∗0) ̸= 0 which is four instructions (not equal is two instructions). However, it is
actually faster to use msb((D b&M) b=M) = 1X because it avoids the negation. It achieves the same since
only one bit is set in M which means that (D b& M) b= 0 or (D b& M) b= M. Thus, if all X elements agrees
with M, then there is a match.
There exists an instruction called vptest that does something equivalent to msbs((D b&M) b̸=0X∗w) =
0X in a single instruction with a latency of 5 which is the same as the latency for msb((D b& M) b= M) =
1X. Some sources state that vptest has a latency of just 3 clock cycles but it is a bit uncertain so
msb((D b& M) b= M) = 1X will be used since all sources agree on the latency for that.
59
==PAGE:61==
As shown in Table 13.6, the total latency per iteration is t + 16 clock cycles where t is the latency
for subtraction. To sum up, Basic Subtraction (match03a) has a latency chain of 15 + 16 = 31 clock
cycles and Thorup Subtraction (match04a) has a latency chain of 13 + 16 = 29 where the subtraction is
the clear bottleneck. Specialized Subtraction (match05a) is clearly the fastest of the three with a latency
chain of just 5 + 16 = 21 clock cycles.

13.9
Latency Chain of Basic Subtraction with Carry (match03b)
In order to determine the most efficient implementation of Basic Subtraction with carry, two different
implementations are considered. The first option, presented in Table 13.8, closely follows the version
presented in section 9.2.2 with the same optimizations as in section 13.4. The second option, presented
in Table 13.9, represents the carry as an integer (0 or 1) to avoid the expensive shuffle in the first option
60
==PAGE:62==
to move the carry from the last element to the first element. However, it then needs to use msbs and cmp
to extract the carry by checking if the 3rd bit is set (≥8) which increases the latency chain for the carry.
On the other hand, the carry can be used to choose between the very fast branch with carry = 0 that
calculates the first two carries in one operation as in section 13.4 and the slower branch with carry = 1
that can only shave off one operation but it avoids the expensive shuffle. Recall that cmp is the latency
for branching.
Looking at the latency chains, there is not much difference. The first option is the fastest with a
latency chain of 17 clock cycles. The second option is slowest when carry = 1 with a latency chain of
19 clock cycles. However, the only the result of the subtraction is needed right after the procedure while
the carry is first needed in the next iteration. In that case, the second option has a latency chain of 15
in the best case and 17 in the worst case. Thus, the second option should be faster.

13.12
Latency Chain of match03b, match04b and match05b
Like with the previous versions with multiple words, the inner matching loop in Table 13.12 is analyzed
first. The subtraction carry is either a vector or an integer that is 0 or 1. In the second case, SubCarry
should be replaced with subCarry. It is very similar to the implementation of match02b in section 12.5
except the vector operators, the shl1or1′ procedure and the subtraction procedures with carry are used.
The inner loop has a latency chain of 12 + tr where tr is the latency chain for calculating the result of
the subtraction.
It is now possible to analyze the matching loop in Table 13.13. It first sets the initial carries where
only one of SubCarry and subCarry is needed. The vector carries can be set with 0 latency by having
the those in another vector register and then move them to the vector register that holds the carry.
For simplicity, the latency of 1 for initializing subCarry is ignored. Checking for a match is performed
by checking if the last vector in D[[index4(i −1)]] matches the vector M. The latency chain for Basic
Subtraction with carry (match03b) has the worst case a latency chain of 29⌈L/(4w)⌉+ 5 clock cycles.
Thorup Subtraction with carry (match05b) is slightly better with a latency chain of 26⌈L/(4w)⌉+5 clock
cycles while Specialized Subtraction with carry (match05b) only has a latency chain of 18⌈L/(4w)⌉+ 5
clock cycles.

14
Implementation in AVX-512
AVX-512 [13] allows to work on 512-bits in parallel and thus allows to work on twice as many elements
compared to AVX2. In addition, AVX-512 has 32 vector registers and supports all operations as opposed
to AVX2. This means that all of the detours tricks mentioned in section 13.1 are not needed in AVX-
512. In particular, AVX-512 includes unsigned comparison, the mask2vec function and wider support for
shuffle. AVX-512 also includes some advanced features that can reduce the number of instructions needed.
However, AVX-512 has some difference compared to the operators described in section 7.1 which will be
addressed before looking at the latencies and throughputs. Finally, the implementation of match06x,
match07x and match08x in AVX-512 are analyzed where the focus is on how to utilize the advanced
features to get lower latencies compared to AVX2.
14.1
Differences in AVX-512
The following sections focus on the differences between the SIMD model presented in section 7.1 and
AVX-512.
14.1.1
Masking
The most significant difference is that AVX-512 allows do masking in almost all operations. It allows to
zero out elements or blend in elements while performing an operation. In order to do this, 8 mask registers
have been introduced to contain the masks to control the masking. There exists simple operations on
these registers such as addition, bit shifts and logical operators. However, most of the operations on the
mask registers are slower that on the normal registers but they might be faster to use than moving data
back and fort between normal registers and mask registers.
As a consequence of mask registers, all comparison operations now generate one bit per vector element
which are collected in a mask register. So for instance, if one want to check for equality between A and
B, then it corresponds to msbs(A b= B). Hence, mask2vec needs to be used to obtain the same result as
in the SIMD model and AVX2.
14.1.2
Ternary Logic
AVX-512 also introduces the possibility to perform ternary logic, that is any bitwise logical operation
with three operands. It is equally fast as bitwise logic with two operators. Thus, this can halve the
latency whenever two bitwise logical operations depends directly on each other. Therefore, this function
is very useful for the implementations. It is defined as follows:
ternaryLogic : vec × vec × vec × op →vec
ternaryLogic(A, B, C, op) := op(A, B, C)
Here, A, B and C are vectors while op is the operation to perform. So if the operation D | (D & I) should
be performed, then one option is to describe it as ternaryLogic(D, D, I, A|(B &C)). In the code snippets
below, the logical operation will be the same as the one described in the Intel Optimization Reference
Manual [23] (Volume 2, Section 5.1) which might not look as the same operations that it replace. Note
that the instruction overwrites the A-argument. It has been considered for the implementation but it
will be ignored here for simplicity.
65
==PAGE:67==
14.1.3
The shuffle and blend Functions
AVX-512 only supports full shuffles using vectors.
Like in AVX2, it is also possible to exploit byte
shift to perform a limited shuffle where only the 64-bit version shuffle[6, z, 4, z, 2, z, 0, z] will be used for
optimizations. Now, blend only accepts a mask register as the control argument.
With the introduction of mask registers, it is also possible to perform blend together with almost all
operations. However, this feature will only be used together with shuffle since it is often there where it
makes sense to use. This function will be called shuffleBlend where two rows will be used to describe the
control vector: The top row tells the indexes from where to pick the element from and the bottom row
tells which of the two vectors. For instance shuffleBlend[ 6
0
5
0
4
0
3
0
2
0
1
0
0
0
0
1] says that the 0th element should
be the 0th element from the second vector while the 7th element should be the 6th element from the first
vector. Note that shuffleBlend like ternaryLogic overwrites an argument but there are two variants of
the instruction that either overwrites one of the vectors to shuffle or the control vector. For simplicity,
this will also be ignored.
14.1.4
Manual Comparison
Looking a bit ahead in Table 14.1, it is noticed that all comparison operators now has a latency of 4 or
6 clock cycles to get the result as a vector. It is possible to perform A b>u B for all element sizes, except
8-bit elements, with a latency of 3 by using the following non-obvious sequence of operations:
procedure graterThanu(A : vec, B : vec) : vec
SubBA ←B b−A
GT ←ternaryLogic(A, B, SubBA, B ? A & C : A | C)
return GT c
>> (w −1) // Zero-extend to get 1 and sign-extend to get −1.
Listing 14.1: Procedure to perform unsigned greater than where the result ends as a vector rather than
a bitmask.
First, the subtraction B b−A is performed as the comparison. In case A b>u B, the subtraction will
underflow and thus an underflow flag for each element would give the result of A b>u B. However, there
is no underflow flag so the idea is to perform (w −1)-bit subtraction such that the MSB, the (w −1)’th
bit, contains the underflow flag. However, the MSBs should still be taken into account for the unsigned
comparison. If msb(Ai) = msb(Bi) then the MSBs cancel each other out and thus if msb(Bi −Ai) = 1
then it is because the (w −1)-bit subtraction Bi −Ai underflowed. Hence, the result in this case should
be msb(Bi −Ai). In case msb(Ai) = 1 and msb(Bi) = 0, then Ai >u Bi and the result should be true (1).
Otherwise msb(Ai) = 0 and msb(Bi) = 1 which implies that Bi >u Ai and thus the result should be false
(0). Note that the result for the last two cases corresponds to msb(Ai). Hence, if msb(Ai) = msb(Bi)
then the result is msb(Bi −Ai) and otherwise msb(Ai). Now notice that this actually corresponds to the
operation in ternaryLogic when msb(Ai) = A, msb(Bi) = B and msb(Bi −Ai) = C:
A = B ? C : A
A ̸= B ? A : C
A ⊕B ? A : C
(A ⊕B =⇒A) & (∼(A ⊕B) =⇒C)
(Continues on next page.)
66
==PAGE:68==
(∼(A ⊕B) | A) & ((A ⊕B) | C)
(∼(A ⊕1) | A) & ((A ⊕1) | C)
(∼(A) | A) & (A | C)
A | C
(∼(A ⊕0) | A) & ((A ⊕0) | C)
(∼(∼A) | A) & (∼A | C)
(A | A) & (∼A | C)
A & (∼A | C)
A & C
(B =⇒(A | C)) & (∼B =⇒(A & C))
B ? A | C : A & C
Hence, the ternaryLogic operation actually calculates A b>u B at the MSB, the (w −1)’th bit, in every
element! Note that the result of the other bits are of no interest. The only thing that remains is to
perform a right bit shift of w −1 bits in each element. To get 0w or 1w = −1 as in AVX2, signed
right shift ( c
>>s) that sign-extends should be used to replicate the result to all bits in each element.
Alternatively, unsigned right shift ( c
>>u) that zero-extends can be used to get 0w or 0w−11. Note that
signed greater, where the meaning of the sign bit is opposite, is achieved by swapping the vectors A and
B in ternaryLogic such that it becomes GT ←ternaryLogic(B, A, SubBA, B ? A & C : A | C). These
methods for performing greater than is only efficient in AVX-512 due to ternaryLogic that allows to do
all the logical operations in a single instruction.
Equality also has a significantly higher latency in AVX-512 compared to AVX2. It is also possible to
get it down to a latency chain of 3 for all element sizes, except 8-bit, with the following steps:
procedure equal(A : vec, B : vec) : vec
SubAB ←A b−B
SubBA ←B b−A
EQ ←ternaryLogic(0X∗w, SubAB, SubBA, ∼(C | B))
return EQ c
>> (w −1) // Zero-extend to get 1 and sign-extend to get −1.
Listing 14.2: Procedure to perform unsigned greater than where the result ends in vector elements rather
than in a bitmask.
First of all, note that −(Ai −Bi) = −Ai +Bi = Bi −Ai. In case Ai = Bi, then Ai −Bi = Bi −Ai = 0.
Otherwise, then Ai −Bi ̸= 0 and Bi −Ai ̸= 0 which means that one of them most be negative when
seeing them as signed integers and thus must have the sign bit set. Hence, if msb(Ai −Bi) = 0 and
msb(Bi −Ai) = 0 is the case, then both subtractions must be zero and thus Ai = Bi.
Thus the
result should be (∼msb(Ai −Bi)) & (∼msb(Bi −Ai)) ≡∼(msb(Bi −Ai) | msb(Ai −Bi)) as reflected
in ternaryLogic. Note that 0X∗w can be any value. Like with unsigned greater than, the result in the
(w −1)’th bit should then be right shifted to obtain 0 or 1/ −1.
For 8-bit elements, it is possible to use 8-bit subtraction and any element size for unsigned right bit
shift by 7 bits. However, bitwise-and with (071)X is needed to remove any bits that are shifted in from
the other elements. Thus, this uses one additional operating that brings the latency up to 4 clock cycles.
Note that signed right shift cannot be used because 1-bits are only shifted in for every second (16-bit),
forth (32-bit) or eighth (64-bit) element due to incorrect size for the bit shift.
67
==PAGE:69==

14.3
Latency Chain of shl1or1
Table 14.3 shows the implementation of shl1or1 in AVX-512. Compared to the AVX2 version, the only
difference is that the shuffleBlend function is used. It rotates the carries as before and then uses the
blend option to replace the carry from the last element with 0631. This eliminates the bitwise-or with
05111. The total latency is reduced by one to 5 clock cycles compared to the AVX2 version.

14.4
Latency Chain of Basic Subtraction (match06a)
The implementation of Basic Subtraction in AVX-512 in Table 14.4 is similar to the AVX2 version except
the bitwise operations have been fused into ternaryLogic and there are now 8 elements instead of 4. This
leads to a latency chain of 25 clock cycles which is just 10 clock cycles more for twice as many elements.
It is possible to use shuffleBlend to reduce the number of operations as seen in Table 14.5 by merging
the blend and shuffle operations.
However, then the gain of using byte shift is gone which gives an
addition latency of 5 clock cycles. It is possible to use byte shift and blend like in Table 14.4 to regain the
5 clock cycles. In addition, using shuffleBlend also dramatically increases the number of vector constants
needed. On the other hand, the first version using blend only needs one vector constant but 7 mask
constants. It is possible to use a single mask constant and then shift it by one (or add it to itself) with
a latency of 4, but that would slow down the calculation because the mask might be needed after just
2 clock cycles. In that case, it is more efficient to load a constant to a normal register and move it to a
mask register (latency of 1 + 1 = 2).

14.5
Latency Chain of Thorup Subtraction (match07a)
Table 14.7 shows the implementation of Thorup Subtraction where the carries are calculated in a mask
register with a latency chain of 7. This results in a total latency chain of 14 clock cycles.
However, it is actually faster to move gt and eq to a normal registers since it is possible to use lea
and xor like in AVX2 with a latency of just 2 and then move the result back to a mask register. The
latency is 3 + 1 = 4 for moving it forth and back which gives a latency chain of 6 for the carries and thus
it is possible to get down to a latency chain of 11 clock cycles as seen in Table 14.8.

14.6
Latency Chain of Specialized Subtraction (match08a)
Comparisons in AVX-512 have a latency of 3 plus a latency of 1 for turning it into a vector for 64-bit
elements while bit shifts have a latency of 1. This means that GT is most efficiently computed as B c
>>uO.
This gives the implementation in Table 14.9 with a latency chain of just 5 clock cycles like in AVX2.

14.7
Latency Chain of match06a, match07a and match08a
Table 14.10 shows the implementation of the matching loop in AVX-512 that is very similar to the one in
AVX2. The first difference is the usage of ternaryLogic in D ←ternaryLogic(D, F, Diff, B ? A : (A|C))
72
==PAGE:74==
to shave off 1 latency. Note that B ? A : (A|C) ≡A|(C &∼B) when considering individual bits because
if B = 1 then A | (C & ∼1) ≡A | (C & 0) ≡A | 0 ≡A and if B = 0 then A | (C & ∼0) ≡A | (C & 1) ≡A | C.
Hence, ternaryLogic(D, F, Diff, B ? A : (A | C)) is just the way to perform D | (Diff & ∼F) with
ternaryLogic.
The second difference is checking for a match where the vptestmq instruction is used because it
performs three operations in one go. However, the result is placed in a mask register and therefore needs
to be moved to a normal register for branching.
It is possible to save one clock cycle more by inlining shl1or1 and combine the last bitwise-or (Shiftedb|
ShiftedCarries) with the bitwise-and (Temp b& B[Tj]) into ternaryLogic. Note that this is only done
in match08c and match08d because the compiler did not make that optimization when automatically
inlining shl1or1.
The latency chain is 40 clock cycles for match06a, 27 for match07a and 21 for match08a. Clearly, the
version with Specialized Subtraction is fastest. The version with Thorup Subtraction is now considerably
faster than Basic Subtraction – mainly because Basic Subtraction should now handle 8 elements instead
of just 4 elements.

14.8
Latency Chain of shl1or1′ with Carry
There are only two differences in the implementation of shl1or1′ with carry in Table 14.11 compared to
the version without carry in Table 14.3. The first difference is the Carry vector argument and returning
the new vector carry, which should be Carries since RotatedCarries is not calculated due to the usage
of shuffleBlend and ShiftedCarries does not contain the carry for the next element. Carries contains
the carry for the next element as the last (7th) element. This leads to the second change which is that
the first (0th) element gets its carry from the last element (instead of the first element) of the second
vector. Note that the initial Carry then should be 06310448 to have a 1 in the last element. This give a
total latency chain of 5 clock cycles like the non-carry variant.

with an additional latency of 3. As seen in the non-carry variant, it could best payoff to use shuffle and

14.10
Latency Chain of Thorup Subtraction with Carry (match07b)
Note that carries in Table 14.13 are calculated and stored using 16-bit (not 8-bit as previously) mask
register instructions such that an eventual overflow bit is preserved and can be used as the carry for the
next element. It has a latency chain of 15 clock cycles.
Like in section 14.5, it is possible to calculate the carries in a normal register with an additional
latency of 4 but faster operations. This again gives an improvement as seen in Table 14.14. Note that the
second variant stores the carry in a normal register and thus have to move it to a mask register before
using mask2vec. This reduces the latency chain of the result by 1 to 12 clock cycles (and the carry has
a latency chain of 11 clock cycles).

14.11
Latency Chain of Specialized Subtraction with Carry (match08b)
Table 14.15 shows the implementation of Specialized Subtraction with Carry. It should be noted that
S′ also contains which vector to pick the element from and not just the index like in section 9.2.4. In
practice, the 3rd bit tells the vector to select. In case S′
i = 7 = 1112 then the 3rd bit is set to 1 meaning
that S′
i = 11112 such that the carry from the previous vector is selected. Otherwise (S′
i ̸= 7), the 3rd
bit is set to 0 to choose from the first vector. The result and carry both have a latency chain of 5 clock
cycles like the non-carry variant.

14.12
Latency Chain of match06b, match07b and match08b
The inner matching loop in Table 14.16 is the same as the AVX2 counter part except ternaryLogic is
used instead of two successive bitwise operations. Note that the subtraction carry is either a vector or
an integer that is 0 or 1. In the second case, SubCarry should be replaced with subCarry. Further
note that the integer carry might be either in a normal register or a mask register. The inner loop has a
latency chain of tr + 11 where tr is the latency chain for calculating the subtraction result.
It is now possible to analyze the out matching loop in Table 14.17. It firsts sets the initial carries
where only one of SubCarry and subCarry is needed. The vector carries can be set with 0 latency by
having the those in another vector register and then move them to the vector register that holds the
carry. For simplicity, the latency of 1 for initializing subCarry is ignored for both when it is in a normal
register and mask register. Recall that the last vector in D[[index8(i −1)]] is checked against the vector
M in the same way as in the single AVX-512 vector version, see section 14.7. The latency chain for
Basic Subtraction with carry (match06b) is 36⌈L/(8w)⌉+ 7 clock cycles, 23⌈L/(8w)⌉+ 7 clock cycles for
Thorup Subtraction with carry (match07b) and only 16⌈L/(8w)⌉+ 7 for Specialized Subtraction with
carry (match08b).

15
Implementation of Parallel Versions in AVX
This section describes analyzes the implementation of the parallel versions in both AVX2 and AVX-512
like the previous sections.
15.1
Latency Data
For the parallel versions, the latencies will be shown again for the operations that are considered for the
parallel versions. Only the latencies will be listed since the throughput is not used in the analysis. The
latencies will be shown in four columns, one for each element size. The latencies for AVX2 are shown in
Table 15.1 while the latencies for AVX-512 are shown in Table 15.2
15.2
Implementation Alternatives
As previously seen, it is sometimes possible to calculate a value more efficiently by using an equivalent
alternative. This is even more important in the parallel versions because some instructions do not exist for
a specific element size or have larger latency for some element sizes. These challenges will be considered
in the following.

15.2.1
Left Shift by One
As seen in Table 15.1 and Table 15.2, bit shifts by a constant are not supported for 8-bit elements. This
is needed for D << 1. As previously seen, addition (D + D) achieves the same and is supported for all
element sizes. Therefore, addition will be used.
15.2.2
Test for Matches
There is a match when (D&M) ̸= 0 or when (D&M) = M as previously seen. Hence, msbs((D b&M) b̸=0)
or msbs((D b&M) b=M) can be used to check for a match in all parts and extract the results as a bitmask.
In AVX2, msbs((D b& M) b= M) will be used since it avoids the negation and usage of the constant 0X∗w.
In AVX2, there is one problem that has not been considered, namely that msbs is not supported for
16-bit elements. One way to get around this is to use the 8-bit variant that extracts twice as many bits
and zero out the unwanted bits: msbs((D b& M) b= M) & (10)16. However, this means that only every
odd bit (counting starts from bit 0) should be counted in order to determine which part that contains a
79
==PAGE:81==
match. This can be achieved by changing i ←trailing0s(matches) to i ←trailing0s(matches) >> 1 since
it divides the number of trailing zeros by two which effectively means that every second 0-bit is ignored.
In AVX-512, the instruction vptestm[bwdq] is supported for all element sizes and can compute
msbs((D b&M) b̸=0) in a single instruction which is clearly the best option. AVX2 has a similar instruction
(vptest) but it only returns one bit and thus cannot be used.
15.2.3
The Shift Carry
Previously, it was noted that (D[k] << 1) | shiftCarry = (D[k] << 1) + shiftCarry because D[k] << 1
ensures the LSB is not set such that addition and bitwise-or with shiftCarry (0 or 1) give the same.
It can be taken one step further by replacing the addition with subtraction if the shiftCarry is 0w or
1w = −1. This dramatically increases the number of possibilities for computing the carry:
 The first option is to use D[k] c
>> (w −1) with a latency of 1 to extract the MSB (either 0 or 1)
as shiftCarry. It is not supported for 8-bit elements, but it is possible to use any of the larger
element sizes to perform the right shift by 7 and then bitwise-and with (071)X to remove all bits
that are shifted in from the next element.
 Another way to test if the MSB (sign bit) is set, is to test if the bits represents a negative signed
integer using D[k] b<s 0. This gives a shiftCarry of 0 or −1. In AVX2, this is fast for all element
sizes except 64-bit elements. In AVX-512, the comparison is slow for all element sizes and mask2vec
is needed to turn it back to a vector. The manual comparison calculation presented in section 14.1.4
could be used but it is still slower than the first option even with the fix for 8-bit elements.
 It is possible to use the signed maximum function, equality and bitwise-not (bitwise-xor) to achieve
the same as D[k] b<s0. More precisely, the shiftCarry can be computed as b∼( d
maxs(D[k], 0) b=D[k]).
Note that maxs(D[k], 0) = D[k] implies 0 ≤s D[k] and thus negating the result (0w or 1w) with
bitwise-not achieves 0 >s D[k]. However, it is not supported for 64-bit elements in AVX2 and is
slow in AVX-512.
 The minimum function can be used in a similar way as the maximum function: b∼(d
mins(D[k], 0) b=0).
 It is possible to negate the result in two previous options by adding 1 instead of using bitwise-not.
Observe that 0 + 1 = 1 and −1 + 1 = 0. Hence, it is possible to obtain 0 or 1 as shiftCarry which
also allows using bitwise-or, but it has no advantages other than that.
 It is also possible to take the absolute value of the second option to get a carry of 0 or 1 but it just
adds an extra operation.
In AVX2, the best option for all element sizes except 8-bit elements is to use D[k] c
>> (w −1) with a
latency of 1. For all element sizes, D[k] b<s 0 can be used with a latency of 1 for all except 64-bit elements
where the latency is 3. Therefore, D[k] b<s 0 will be used for the 8-bit version.
In AVX-512, only the first option (with the fix for 8-bit elements) is useful since it has a latency of
1 −2 while all (other) options using comparison have a latency of 3+.
15.2.4
The Subtraction Carry
Like the shiftCarry, the subCarry can either be added (having 0 or −1) or subtracted (having 0 or 1).
The straight forward way is to compute the subCarry (having 0 or −1) as (cI b−subCarry) b>u cF. It is
possible to use the unsigned maximum/minimum functions in a similar fashion as for the shiftCarry.
80
==PAGE:82==
It is not possible to avoid a comparison. Thus, (cI b−subCarry) b>u cF is the most efficient way in
AVX2 since it has a latency of 1+2 = 3 while using the maximum/minimum functions will have a latency
of 1 + 1 + 1 + 1 = 4. However, note that 64-bit elements cannot use the maximum/minimum functions
and the comparison has a latency of additional 2 clock cycles.
Since a comparison cannot be avoided, the manual computation of greater than in AVX-512 (see
section 14.1.4) is the best choice for all element sizes. It has latency of 2 except for 8-bit elements that
has a latency of 3 because it shares calculations with the subtraction.
15.3
Inserting Elements into Vector
The final thing to consider is how tasks are generated. More precisely, how X elements are inserted
into a vector. This has not been covered yet because it is quite cumbersome since it involves a mix of
instructions from SSE (128-bit registers), AVX2 (256-bit registers) and AVX-512 (512-bit registers) [13].
The parallel AVX-512 version using 8-bit elements (match10a08) needs to insert 64 elements into a vector
which GCC uses 64+32 = 96 instructions to do, disregarding all the operations except those for creating
the vector. The reason is that it is only possible to insert elements from normal registers into the first
128 bits. Here, it is most efficient to insert 32 or 64 bits at the lowest 32/64 bits. It may be a bit more
costly to insert 8, 16, 32 or 64 bits at any position in the first 128 bits. Notably, it is cheaper to perform
a special kind of shuffleblend within the first 128 bits than inserting at an arbitrary position in the first
128 bits. Therefore, the most efficient pattern to insert X elements is to do the following:
 Insert X/2 w-bit elements at the lowest position (0) in the first 128 bits. There are now X/2 vectors
with one element each. Note that 8 or 16 bits can be inserted as 32 bits because the upper bits are
zero (or overwritten later). The latency is 2cde (1a) and the throughput is 1abcde for the instructions
vmov[dq].
 Insert the other X/2 w-bit elements at offset w (position 1), one element in each of the X/2 vectors
from the previous step. There are now X/2 vectors with two elements each. The latency is 2ac (3d)
and the throughput is 1ade (2c) for the instructions vpinsr[bwdq].
 Use the special kind of shuffleblend to combine vectors two and two recursively until the elements
take up 128 bits.
The latency is 1abcde and the throughput is 0.5abde (1c) for the instructions
vpunpcklwd (16 to 32 bits), vpunpckldq (32 to 64 bits) and vpunpcklqdq (64 to 128 bits).
 Join vectors two and two by inserting the low 128 bits from one vector into the high 128 bits of
the other vector. In case of the AVX2 versions, only two vectors are joined into one vector. The
latency is 3abcde and the throughput is 1abcde for the instruction vinserti128.
 Only AVX-512: Join two 256-bit vectors by inserting the low 256 bits from one vector into the high
256 bits of the other vector. The latency is 3abcde and the throughput is 1abcde for the instruction
vinserti64x4.
This gives dependency chains that form a binary tree. Returning to the example with 64×8-bit elements,
the latency chain is:
 Latency of 2 + 2 = 4 for getting 32 vectors of two 8-bit elements.
 Latency of 1 to get 16 vectors of 4 × 8-bit elements.
 Latency of 1 to get 8 vectors of 8 × 8-bit elements.
81
==PAGE:83==
 Latency of 1 to get 4 vectors of 16 × 8-bit elements.
 Latency of 3 to get 2 vectors of 32 × 8-bit elements.
 Latency of 3 to get 1 vector with 64 × 8-bit elements.
Hence, the latency chain is just 13 clock cycles. Clearly, this kind of dependency pattern is not limited
by latency but throughput. However, the last two steps are limited by latency because they only contain
1 −2 instructions and the latency is 3 clock cycles. All instructions have a throughput of 1 except the
special kind of shuffleblend that has a reciprocal throughput of 0.5 and thus two of those instructions can
run at the same time if they are independent which is the case if the sequence is performed optimally.
Note that obtaining 2 × 256-bit vectors in AVX-512 takes 4 clock cycles because the throughput is 1 and
thus the second vector can first start being created 1 clock cycle after the first vector. The total number
of clock cycles needed for inserting X elements into one vector is then:

The sequence for inserting the X elements into the vector might include memory access and/or moving
data between registers. These will likely scale proportional to the number of clock cycles found above,
but these factors are for simplicity ignored. However, the offsets (j + i ∗distance where 0 ≤i < X) into
T still need to be calculated. These can be calculated as X independent calculations using multiplication
and sometimes be optimized with shl and/or lea.
It is also possible to start with j and then add
distance one by one X times. The second option is easiest to analyze (it has X latency) and requires less
instructions. Memory access is still disregarded but might have a much larger impact here than in the
sequential versions because X different positions are accessed in T and there are many temporal values
that might not fit in the limited amount of registers. There are additional tricks that appears faster as
described in section 16.1 but these will be left out of the analysis.
