16
Implementation Optimizations
This section will describe some further tricks and discoveries found during the final optimizations of the
different versions. The effect of these cannot be fully analyzed as in the previous sections. Note that all
performance measurements in this section was performed using an older version of GCC (7.5.0) on an
older CPU (Intel Core 8th generation, Kaby Lake Refresh [12]) that only supports AVX2.
16.1
Inserting Elements into Vectors
As described in section 15.3, there are other possibilities than just inserting X elements into a vector.
An example is to combine 8 × 8-bit elements into one 64-bit register using left bit shift and bitwise-or in
order to have less elements to combine. It requires more instructions but might be faster in some cases
due to better throughput (see Table 12.1). However, that was strangely enough not the change that gave
the largest performance impact.
In the match09b and match10b versions, there was initially a loop for calculating the offsets in T and
a loop for creating the vector c ⃗B where elements were inserted in groups of 64-bits by combing elements
with left bit shift and bitwise-or. Notably, the data type was set to the type of a vector register which
made GCC store each element in memory and then afterwards load it. This may not sound that bad but
it seems to trigger stalls due to different sizes of the stores and loads which prevents store-forwarding [23]
(section 3.6.4). In addition, the CPU also has to wait for the data to be ready. For the 64-bit parallel
version match09b, the time for the first 25 PROSITE patterns on 50 MB text was 5.4 seconds. If an
array (64-bit entries) was used instead and the elements were then loaded one-by-one into a vector, the
time usage goes down to 3.6 seconds. The elements are still stored in memory and then loaded, but
store-forwarding can now be used since the same addresses and sizes are used. Furthermore, unrolling
the loops reduces the time to 2.4 seconds which is 3 seconds less! The 8-bit versions only went from
3.2 seconds to 2.0 seconds, but it is still a major improvement. In any case, this shows the effects of
store-forwarding and the gain of unrolling loops which also avoids branch mis-predictions.
16.2
Assembly Optimization
After implementing all the versions ending on a and b, the assembly output generated by GCC was
considered. The versions match00, match01x and match02x were not considered because GCC would
probably be able to do a better job in optimizing that because of its knowledge of registers and instructions
in the x86-64 instruction set. The preprocessing was neither considered for optimizations since it contains
a lot of branching and GCC would also do a better job here because no vector instructions are needed
except for the final loop in the preprocessing in the vector versions (match03x-match08x) but here GCC
made the wanted assembly code.
When considering the matching phase of the versions that utilizes AVX vector instructions, it was
discovered that the compiler often used vector instructions with a memory operand rather than a register.
This adds the latency of a vector load instruction before the actual instruction which is quite expensive
as it costs 7-8 clock cycles. This was the case for all constants as well as the bitmasks I, F, M, D,
S and O. This makes sense for the versions ending with b because they each need to use ⌈L/(Xw)⌉
different vectors per character in T. However, it does not make sense for the versions ending on a because
there are enough vector registers available, namely 16 vector registers in AVX2 and 32 vector registers in
AVX-512. Note that the Intel Optimization Reference Manual [23] (section 3.5.1.11) recommends that
constants may be kept in memory to minimize register pressure which might be why GCC does as it
does. It was also noted that D was loaded in the start of each iteration and stored in the end of each
iteration instead of just being kept in a vector register. In addition, the compiler also inserted some extra
87
==PAGE:89==
move operations due to AVX-512 instructions that have three input operands where one of them is the
output operand. In particular, the ternaryLogic function has the first input parameter (A) as output
parameter (had been taking into account) and shuffleBlend assigns the result to the second vector or the
control argument vector which in some cases are constants. Vector move instructions are free in terms of
latency but gives more work to the instruction decoder which might make it slower. Hence, optimizing
the matching phase with assembly can make sure all bitmasks and constants are kept in vector registers
and that unnecessary moves are avoided by eventually flipping some arguments.
It was decided only to optimize the Specialized Subtraction (match05a and match08a) and 64-bit
parallel versions (match09a and match10a) as these were the most promising to compete with match02a.
Details about the optimizations will be left out as the overall ideas are described above. The actual
improvements can be see in section 17. However, some interesting observations during the implementation
of match05c are presented in the following. Noted that this was performed on the older Kaby Lake Refresh
CPU only supporting AVX2 with an older version (7.5.0) of GCC:
To test the performance, the first 50 patterns in the PROSITE database was tested with 50 MB of text
each. match05a using the (slow) Specialized Subtraction with U (rather than O) used 18.4 seconds. Using
the version with O only used 16.2 seconds (−2.2 seconds). Then it was tried to specify that D should
be in a specific vector register without using any other assembly statements. It worked and interestingly
enough the time was now 11.6 seconds which is another 4.6 seconds saved. However, GCC stated in its
documentation that there are no guarantee that this would work without doing all operations with D in
assembly. The same trick was also applied to I, F, M, S and O by making additional variables in vector
registers when their values had been set in the preprocessing, but this time it did not work because it
still generated memory operands. Therefore, all vector operations in the matching phase were done in
assembly which got rid of the memory operands. Now, you might expect that it shaved off some seconds,
but it turned out only to be 0.2 seconds (11.4 seconds in total)! Hence, the CPU is either smart enough to
preload the vectors into vector registers in each iteration (most likely), it knows that the value is already
present in a vector register (unlikely) or the memory cache is so efficient that you almost cannot notice
it. In any case, doing all in assembly is the safest to avoid tricks that it might only work in the older
version of GCC.
During the implementation in assembly, a weird problem was encountered where one of the registers
at some point changed to what appeared as a random value. It turned out to be due to usage of memcpy in
the doubling array used to store matches. Replacing it with a plain loop fixed the problem but increased
the time to 11.5 seconds (+0.1 seconds). Hence, memcpy must sometimes use the vector registers to copy
data. This argument is strengthen by the Intel Optimization Reference Manual [23] (section 15.16.3.1)
that describes a heuristic to use SIMD registers for memcpy. It was later discovered that calling a function
that follows the Abstract Binary Interface (ABI) standard, such as memcpy, it is allowed to freely use
any vector registers meaning that the caller would have to save and restore all used vector registers each
time memcpy is called [27]. This also applies to malloc and free but they do not appear to use vector
registers and there is no obvious reason why they should use them. Therefore, it should be enough just to
replace the call to memcpy to avoid having to save the vector registers. The problem was first discovered
in match05c but here it seemed to help by just using another register. It was first in the implementation
of match09c that the cause of the problem was discovered. On Linux, none of the vector registers have
to be saved and restored by the called ABI function. However on Windows, there are 6 vector registers
where only the first 128 bits need to be saved and restored by the called ABI function. None of the
PROSITE patterns have maximum lengths longer than 128 so that is why using another vector register
appeared to help. It should be noted that the implementation is made in GCC installed on Ubuntu in
Windows Subsystem for Linux (WSL) where it appears to be the Windows ABI version that is used. It
might be due to the ABI conventions that GCC uses memory operands in the vector instructions and
88
==PAGE:90==
loads/stores D in start/end of the loop (before reporting matches). [27]
16.3
Lookup Table in Vector Registers
So far, all versions store the lookup table B in memory. However, the table could also be stored in vector
registers if the table is sufficiently small and there are many vector registers available. In AVX-512,
there are 32 vector registers capable of storing 512 bits each, but only 16 vector registers of 256 bits on
CPU’s that only supports AVX2. The size of the table B depends on the version and the size of the
alphabet. In the implementation, patterns from the PROSITE database [28] is matched with strings
of protein patterns. There are 21 different proteins that are represented by one character each from A
to Y except the characters B, J, O and X. Hence, there are 21 entries in B. All versions ending on b
uses multiple words/vectors and thus each entry can be arbitrarily large such that it cannot be stored in
vector registers. The sequential versions ending on a need one vector register per entry meaning that it
is possible to store all in the AVX-512 versions. The parallel versions ending on a only need one vector
element meaning that the 64-bit versions need 4 times fewer registers in AVX2 and 8 times fewer registers
in AVX-512 (both rounded up). Note that these optimizations are very specific to the how the alphabet is
represented in binary so it might be considerably slower for other alphabets. The focus will be on storing
the lookup table in AVX-512 registers for the versions match08c and match10c64 to see how much it is
possible to optimize. These will result in the versions ending on d.
16.3.1
One Character to One Entry (match08d)
In the sequential AVX-512 versions, one character is looked up in B and the corresponding vector is
loaded from memory into a vector register.
If each entry in B is stored in a vector register then a
character c should result in a move instruction from the vector register representing B[c] to the a vector
register that is going to be used for the bitwise-and (using ternaryLogic) with B[c]. Instructions cannot
have a variable register, so a jump table (switch statement) is needed to select an instruction that moves
the correct vector register to the destination based on the character. And that is it. Note that this might
be slower than the latency of 8 clock cycles for loading from memory because the whole lookup table
will likely be in the fastest cache and thus avoids additional stalls. On the other hand, a jump table
requires branching which might give stalls if the wrong branch is predicted. Note that there is actually
only 2 vector registers free, because 21 vector registers goes to storing B, 7 vector registers to store the
constants I, F, M, S, O and two constants, 1 vector register to store D and 1 vector register to store the
chosen entry from B. Optimizing the register usage in the matching loop, then there are enough vector
registers for the temporary values.
16.3.2
Many Characters to Many Entries (match10d)
In the parallel AVX-512 versions, X characters are one by one looked up in B and the results are then
inserted one-by-one into a vector. As seen in section 15.3, it is very costly to insert many elements into
a vector and the impact of all the memory accesses have not been considered. Even though the 8-bit
version is the one that might benefit most from storing B in vector registers, its usage is very limited
since it only can handle patterns of maximum length 8. On the other hand, the 64-bit version is the one
that might benefit the least but it has an additional advantage which will be described later.
Character to Vector and Index
With 21 entries in B, it should be possible to store them all in
just 3 vector register with space for 24 elements. This makes it possible to use just two shuffleBlend
operations to being able to select any element from the 3 vectors. For that, an efficient kind of hashing
89
==PAGE:91==
function is needed to turn each 1-byte (8-bit) character into an integer that tells which vector that an
element should be selected from and which element that should be selected. The binary representation of
the characters forms a small range of integers from 65 (A) to 89 (Y) with a few gaps, so the most straight
forward option is to subtract 65 (A) from the characters to get down to a range from 0 to 89 −65 = 24.
There is just one problem, namely that the range contains 25 integers which is one too much to fit within
3 vectors. Saturation arithmetics [21] turns out to save the day. In short, saturation arithmetics make
sure that the result does not wrap around (overflows/underflows). It is noted that the character B is not
used which means that there is a gap after A that can be used. So if it is possible to make A use the spot
for B then the range fits perfectly within 3 vectors. If 66 (B) is subtracted from all characters, then the
integer range is −1 to 23. If the subtraction uses unsigned saturation, then all integers below 0 will be
turned into 0 such that the range becomes 0 to 23 and fits! It is possible to use saturation with 8-bit
elements since the characters only takes up one byte. Now note the binary (5-bit) representations for
each character after the subtraction with unsigned saturation:
I = 00111
H = 00110
G = 00101
F = 00100
E = 00011
D = 00010
C = 00001
A = 00000
Q = 01111
P = 01110
O = 01101
N = 01100
M = 01011
L = 01010
K = 01001
J = 01000
Y = 10111
X = 10110
W = 10101
V = 10100
U = 10011
T = 10010
S = 10001
R = 10000
It is noted that at most three characters (every column) shares the same three least significant bits.
These represents integers from 0 to 7 and thus are perfect as the index of the element to select. Similarly,
every row of character shares the same two most significant bits that represents the integers 0, 1 and 2
which are perfect as selector for the vector in shuffleBlend! This gives rise to creating the following three
vectors:
B0 ←[B[I], B[H], B[G], B[F], B[E], B[D], B[C], B[A]]
B1 ←[B[Q], B[P], 0w, B[N], B[M], B[L], B[K], 0w]
B2 ←[B[Y ], 0w, B[W], B[V ], B[U], B[T], B[S], B[R]]
Loading Characters into Vectors
Before looking at how the actual lookup is performed, the char-
acters first need to be loaded from T. First, two variables are needed:
Indexes ←[7 ∗distance + start, 6 ∗distance + start, . . . , distance + start, start]
state ←7
Indexes corresponds to the indexes used in the first iteration of the parallel version where j = start and
state keeps track of the current state among the possible eight states from 0 to 7 (it will make sense in
a moment). The characters are then loaded in the following way:
if state = 7 then
Ts ←gather(T, Indexes, 1) // 64-bit operation.
Indexes ←Indexs b+ broadcast(8) // 64-bit operation.
state ←0
else
Ts ←Ts c
>>u 8 // 64-bit operation.
state ←state + 1
90
==PAGE:92==
The current loaded characters are stored in Ts. The idea is then to load characters in groups of eight
characters at a time since eight characters fits into each 64-bit element. The AVX-512 gather function
(vpgatherqq) is used to load the groups of 8 characters from T with the indexes given in Indexes scaled
by the factor 1 meaning that Indexes is the offset in bytes (characters). This is actually a hack because
it loads the groups of 8 characters like they were 64-bit integers. This might give problems depending on
whether big-endian or little-endian is used [22]. The x86-64 instruction set architecture use little-endian
which means that the least significant byte of a word is at the smallest memory address. In particular,
this means that the first character (smallest memory address) is the least significant byte (8 bits) of the
64-bit integer. Hence, the first character that should be considered is at the 8 least significant bits and
the last is at the 8 most significant bits. This is good news, since it allows to right shift by 8 bits to move
the next character to the least significant bits. Thus, state tells how many times the characters have
been shifted and new characters should be loaded when the last character is at the eight least significant
bits. The reason why it is very practical to have the current character at the least significant eight bits
in each 64-bit element becomes clear in the next step.
Performing the Lookup
It is now possible to perform the lookup. In the following, the expressions
in curly brackets are masks that are either used to zero out elements (the first operation) or prevent
elements from changing (the last operation):
BIndexes ←saturateu{(071)8}(Ts b−broadcast(66)) // 8-bit operation.
Temp ←shuffleBlend[BIndexes](B2, B1) // 64-bit operation.
bChanges ←msbs(BIndexes <u broadcast(8)) // 64-bit operation.
BIndexes ←BIndexes b+ broadcast(8) // 64-bit operation.
c ⃗B ←shuffleBlend[BIndexes]{bChanges}(Temp, B2) // 64-bit operation.
First, 8-bit subtraction with unsigned saturation is used to calculate the vector and element indexes for
the current characters. The used mask zeros out elements where the bit is 0 which means that all 8-bit
elements except the ones with the current characters are zeroed. If the resulting vector is then seen as
64-bit elements, the upper 56 bits are all 0 while the lower 8-bits contains the result with vector and
element indexes. Hence, these results are now available as 64-bit integers and can be used in shuffleBlend
to select between B2 (10xxx) or B1 (01xxx). Recall that the 3rd bit (counting starts from 0) determines
if it is the first vector (if 0) or the second vector (if 1) that should be selected. Note that characters
that actually need an element from B0 (00xxx) take an element from B2 (10xxx) since the 3rd bit is 0
in both. This means that all elements that need an element from the B0 have a wrong element while
all other elements have the correct element. Therefore, only elements that should select from B0 should
change in the next shuffleBlend. These have the value 00xxx meaning that all indexes that are less than
01000 should be changed and thus have a 1 in the mask bChanges. If shuffleBlend is used with a mask to
keep elements, then all elements that have a corresponding 0 in the mask stays as the first vector given
to shuffleBlend. Unfortunately, all elements that should change have 0 as their 3rd bit, so the 3rd bit
must be flipped which can be achieved by adding (or bitwise-xor) with 060103 = 8. Finally, all elements
that should change have a 1-bit in the mask bChanges and have the 3rd bit set in BIndexes such that
the last shuffleBlend ensures that the correct value from the lookup table have been selected. Note that
broadcast(8) can be replaced with a constant, but broadcast(8) is used for better readability.
Latency Chain Analysis
The only thing that remains is to analyze the latency chain. Section 15.5
showed that the latency chain is t + 12 clock cycles where t is the time for creating c ⃗B. The latency
91
==PAGE:93==
for vpgatherqq is 21e (26c) clock cycles and saturation subtraction has the same latency as subtraction
(1abcde) but according to [18], a mask might increase the latency to 3e clock cycles. The analysis in
Table 16.1 shows that the latency chain is 31 clock cycles for the slow case when vpgatherqq is used and
just 11 clock cycles in the fast case. Hence, the average is (1/8) ∗31 + (7/8) ∗11 = 13.5 clock cycles and
thus the average latency chain is 25.5 clock cycles. This is 9.5 clock cycles faster than the 64-bit parallel
version in AVX-512 (match10a) even when memory access has been taken into account!

17
Experimental Results
This section will present the experimental results. First, the testing methods will be described. After-
wards, the results of the preprocessing time will be shown. Then the performance of the different variants
of the same version mentioned in the implementation sections will be compared to find the best option.
Finally, the results of the performance tests of all versions will be compared to find the best version for
each scenario.
17.1
Testing Methods
To the test the preprocessing of each version, the version were ran 500 000 times for each pattern with
the empty string as the text T. This does not only test the preprocessing, but essential everything except
the matching phase. This might give a higher preprocessing time for the parallel versions since they
concatenate the (empty) results at the end. The matching phase were tested by running the versions
once per pattern with a 50 MB text (fraction) of human DNA as a protein sequence from [29]. All results
will be shown in seconds with one decimal. The actual results have a precision of six decimals.
The tests were performed on the Intel Core i7-1195G7 (11th generation) processor with codename
Tiger Lake (Willow Cove microarchitecture), 12 MB cache and up to 5.00 GHz (likely only up to 2.90
GHz) [11]. Two groups of patterns were tested. The first group was the 1311 PROSITE patterns from
the PROSITE database [28] where 16 patterns were slightly modified by removing their constraint to
only match the start and/or the end of the text. Furthermore, one pattern contained X(0, 1) (uppercase
X ) which was corrected to x(0, 1) (lowercase x). The second group was 322 custom patterns with three
kind of patterns:
 152 patterns of the form A −x(i, j) −A where i ≤j, j ̸= 0 and i, j ∈{62, 126, . . . , 1022}.
 85 patterns of the form [C](i) where |C| ∈{1, 2, 4, 8, 16} and i ∈{1, 64, 128, 192, . . . , 1024}.
 85 patterns of the form {C}(i) where |C| ∈{1, 2, 4, 8, 16} and i ∈{1, 64, 128, 192, . . . , 1024}.
The custom patterns are meant to test the versions that supports longer patterns in a more fair way since
the PROSITE patterns have a maximum length of at most 123. Table 17.1 shows the distribution of the
maximum lengths of patterns.
It was also tested if all versions worked correctly by testing if they gave the expected results on
patterns constructed to test the edge cases. In addition, it was also tested that all versions gave the exact
same number of matches for the performance tests of the matching phase as an extra test. All versions
passed these tests.

17.2
Preprocessing
The main focus in this thesis have been on optimizing the main loop. However, the preprocessing was
actually optimized in section 4 which should be notable in the results. Table 17.2 shows the preprocessing
time of the PROSITE patterns for all the versions supporting a limited maximum length (the versions
ending on a, c and d). The results are divided into all patterns up to a certain maximum length where
some versions cannot handle longer patterns. The first thing to note is that match02a is significantly
faster than match01a for patterns with L ≤64 which shows that the preprocessing optimization really
pays off. All the sequential AVX versions (match03a to match08d) that have the same improvements are
also faster than match01a. However, they are all notably slower than match02a which is because those
versions works on arrays which have additional overhead. Maybe surprisingly, the extra variables that
need to be preprocessed in match05[ac] and match05[acd] do not seem to affect the preprocessing time
at all. On the other hand, the preprocessing time of the parallel AVX versions are significantly larger
than the other versions. However, their time usage include the creating of X arrays for the results and
concatenating them after the matching phase which is the reason why the use significantly more time
on preprocessing (and postprocessing). Clearly, this must be the cause of the increased time usage since
match10a08 is the slowest with X = 64 while match09a08 and match10a16 are around twice as fast and
they both have X = 32.
The preprocessing time for the versions without limitations on the maximum length for the PROSITE
patterns are shown in Table 17.3. The naive algorithm (match00) is clearly the fastest, since it has no
preprocessing and thus its time usage can be seen as the overhead of testing the preprocessing. Again, the
optimizations in the preprocessing pays off as seen on the difference between match01b and match02b,
but the improvement is smaller. As opposed to the versions supporting a limited maximum length, the
sequential AVX2 versions use close to the same time as match01b while the sequential AVX-512 versions
are notably slower. The cause might be due to it takes longer to load 512 bits compared to 256 bits and 64
bits. Again, the parallel AVX versions are the slowest of the same reasons as before. Table 17.4 shows the
preprocessing time of the custom patterns for the versions without limitations on the maximum length. It
shows a quite different picture. match01b is now significantly slower than match02b as expected because
match01b sets one bit at a time while match02b can set up to 64 bits at a time. It should be noted that
the custom patterns tests the patterns where match02b should be able to set as many bits as possible
at a time. The sequential AVX versions are also much closer to the preprocessing time of match02b.
The sequential AVX2 versions are actually faster when the longest patterns are included. The sequential
AVX-512 versions are slower, but are close to match match02b when the longest patterns are included.
The difference is likely in the final preprocessing loop because it takes longer to load vectors compared
to a single array entry. Thus, even longer patterns might make AVX-512 the fastest option but shorter
patterns are best handled by normal instructions. Again, the parallel AVX versions are the slowest.
It should be noted that even though the preprocessing might be slower for some versions, its impact
is likely going to be insignificant when the text is very long because all patterns are preprocessed 500 000
times faster than the shown times.

17.3
Best Variants
Before moving on to the performance tests of the matching phase, the best variants from the implemen-
tation sections should be found. The test were performed by changing the given part in the version used
to test the variant rather than just testing the different options alone as recommended by [24] (section
16.2). The reason is that the performance might depend on the other instructions around the change
because the change might allow/prevent that the changed instructions can run at the same time as other
instructions. The following paragraphs will compare the different variants.
Vector Bit Shift vs Vector Addition
First, the choice between vector left shift by one and addition
in shl1or1 is considered in Table 17.5. Note that the Lat-column shows the calculated latency chain. The
results shows that there are no notable difference between the two options because the difference is so
small that the difference might be external factors. Vector addition is used in future variants of shl1or1
and shl1or1′.
match03a (L ≤256)
Lat
PROSITE
Custom
Shifted ←D c
<< 1
32
460.5
24.1

24.0
Table 17.5: Vector left shift by one bit versus vector addition in shl1or1.
Checking for Matches in AVX2
Next up is to find the best way to check for matches in the sequential
AVX2 versions. The results of the three options are shown in Table 17.6. Again, there are no notable
difference even thought the latency chains varies a bit. match ←! testz(D, M) (vptest) will be used in
all future sequential AVX2 versions.

Element Size in AVX2 Thorup Subtraction
Table 17.7 shows the difference of using 64-bit ele-
ments or 32-bit elements in AVX2 Thorup Subtraction. Recall that vectorized greater than for 64-bit
elements is 2 clock cycles slower than the 32-bit version. This can actually be seen in Table 17.7 where
32-bit variant is 2-3% faster than 64-bit variant. Therefore, 32-bit elements will be used in future variants
of match04x.

Carry Representation in AVX2 Basic Subtraction
The carry could either be represented as an
vector or an integer in AVX2 Basic Subtraction. Using a vector had the disadvantage of using more
expensive vector instructions while the integer carry required more work to obtain the carry and could
result in branch mis-predictions. In addition, the latency chain for the slow path in the integer carry
variant was the same as the latency chain for vector carry variant. However, Table 17.9 shows that the
integer carry variant is the clear winner since it is around 11% faster than the vector carry variant. The
reason why it so much faster could be that the extra work is off the main path and thus can be performed
when it fits best with other instructions. In addition, the first carry is always 0 which triggers the fast
case, so there might be overweight of the runs of the fast case. The integer carry variant will be used in
future match03b variants.
match03b (any L)
Lat
PROSITE
Custom
Vector carry (shuffle)
29⌈L/(4w)⌉+ 5

Table 17.9: Vector carry with shuffle versus integer carry with branching.
Separate Versus Joined shuffle and blend
For Basic Subtraction in AVX-512, it was considered
whether the fastest choice would be to use shuffle and blend separately or join them into shuffleBlend.
The second option should be slower which is confirmed by Table 17.10. However, the difference is smaller
than expected since the latency chains estimates that the second option should be around 12% slower
but it is only around 1%. Besides that, joined shuffleBlend requires more constants but it does not seem
to harm to load them as they can be loaded well in advance. Separate shuffle and blend will be used in
future variants of match06a.
match06a (L ≤256)
Lat
PROSITE

Separate shuffle and blend

684.8

Joined shuffleBlend
46
693.2
73.7
Table 17.10: Separate shuffle and blend versus joined shuffleBlend in AVX-512 Basic Subtraction.
Comparison in AVX-512 Basic Subtraction
Basic Subtraction in AVX-512 will again be consid-
ered, but this time the focus is on making the comparison operations. Table 17.11 shows that it can
best pay off to use the manual comparison calculation that is around 7% faster than using a comparison
instruction followed by mask2vec. The latency chain says that improvement should only be 2-3% so the
reason that the improvement is greater is likely due to the vector subtraction and ternaryLogic instructions
98
==PAGE:100==
(throughput of 0.5) can run at the same time as opposed to the comparison and mask2vec instructions
(throughput of 1). Manual comparison calculations will be used in future variants of match06x.

Table 17.11: Comparison to mask and then use mask2vec versus manual comparison calculation in AVX-
512 Basic Subtraction.
Carry Register in AVX-512 Thorup Subtraction
The next potential improvement to consider
is whether it is best to calculate the carries in a mask register or move it to a normal register and do
the calculations there in AVX-512 Thorup Subtraction. Table 17.12 shows that in match07a, the best
option is to move the carry to a normal register, performing the calculations there and move it back as
expected. It is 5-6% faster than doing it in a mask register. The same is the case for the version with
carry match07b as seen in Table 17.13 where the improvement is even better, namely 11-13%. Hence,
future variants of match07x will be calculating carries in a normal register.

Parallel Versions
Finally, different variants of the parallel versions will be considered.
First, the
best way to check for matches is considered for match09a. This have actually already been covered in
Table 17.6 where there were no difference and Table 17.14 confirms that. matches ←msbs((D b&M) b=M)
will be used for future variants.
The other variations are how the carries are calculated. Table 17.15 and Table 17.16 shows that it
does not really matter how it is calculated since it is off the main dependency chain as discussed for
Table 17.9. The second option in both cases will be used for further variants.

Table 17.16: Comparison of two ways calculate the subtraction carry for the parallel AVX-512 versions.
17.4
Performance
As seen in the previous section, it is possible to get an improvement of up 13% in some cases by choosing
the right instruction sequences without changing the overall version design (without considering assembly
optimization and lookup table in vector registers). As will be seen in the following, choosing the right
algorithm has an even larger impact. First, the results for the versions supporting a limited maximum
length will be discussed. Afterwards, the results for the versions supporting any maximum length will be
discussed. Finally, the most promising versions from both categories will be compared to find the best
versions in each case.
100
==PAGE:102==
17.4.1
Limited Maximum Length
Table 17.17 and Table 17.18 shows the results for the versions supporting a limited maximum length.
Note that the first row shows the time for just iterating through the 50 MB text once per pattern and
report the same number of matches as the versions find for a given pattern. The results will first be
discussed for smaller groups of similar versions.
x86-64 Versions
As opposed to the preprocessing, there is no notable difference between match01a
and match02a which shows that the preprocessing time for short patterns (64 ≤L) is not important.
Furthermore, it also shows that despite match02a has a 22% lower latency chain than match01a, they
use the same time.
Sequential AVX2 Versions
Among the sequential AVX2 versions ending on a, match05a is clearly
the fastest as expected from the latency chain. The latency chains predicted that match04a should come
in second but it is 11-13% slower than match03a. This might be because the broadcast function in AVX2
is much slower than the 3 clock cycles that were based on the assumption that store-forwarding would
give 0 latency for loading the data to broadcast. Optimizing match05a in assembly gave match05c. As
seen from the results, match05c is 30-35% faster than match05a which is a huge improvement where the
main improvement comes from memory access by eliminating the loading and storing of D.
Sequential AVX-512 Versions
The sequential AVX-512 versions ending on a shows a different pic-
ture. Here, the results actually reflects the latency chains because match06a is slowest, match07a is in
the middle and match08a is the fastest of the three. Furthermore, the latency chains almost reflects the
time differences. The latency chains states that match07a should be 29% slower than match08a while
match06a should be 90% slower than match08a. The tests shows that match07a is 36-40% slower than
match08a while match06a is 104-115% slower than match08a, so the actual differences are larger. This
might be because the comparisons in the subtraction for match06a and match07a cannot run in parallel as
assumed by the latency chain calculations. Like for the AVX2 versions, optimizing match08a in assembly
gives an significant improvement, namely a speed up of 32-34%. However, storing the table B in vector
registers as match08d is clearly a bad idea, since it is more than twice as slow as match08c that match08d
builds on top off. Hence, it is much better to relay on a random memory access (RAM) cache than a
jump table and branch-prediction for accessing entries in B.
Parallel AVX2 Versions
For the parallel versions, it should be noted that the latency chains does not
tell the whole story because each version performs a different number of iterations. Therefore, the product
of the Lat-column and Iter-column gives the expected relative performance (disregarding memory access).
In all cases, the version ending on a that supports the smallest maximum length and highest amount
of parallelism is the fastest. Optimizing match09a64 in assembly (match09c64) gives an improvement
for the PROSITE patterns, but it is smaller compared to improvement of the sequential AVX versions.
The improvement is a 22% speed up. However, match09a64 and match09c64 use the same time for the
custom patterns which only covers the 22 patterns that have a lot of matches (over 1/3 of all matches
among the 322 custom patterns).
Parallel AVX-512 Versions
For the parallel AVX-512 versions, the results are a bit different. Here,
match10a16 is slightly faster than match10a08 and thus match10a16 is the fastest for the PROSITE
patterns with maximum lengths of at most 16. It is likely due to the extra bitwise-and operation in
the carry calculations in match10a08. Similarly, match10a64 is also faster than match10a32, but it is

==PAGE:104==
unclear why this is the case. The assembly optimized version match10c64 is 7% faster than match10a64.
The version that also stores B in a vector registers and load 8 × 8 characters at a time (match10d64) is
almost twice as fast as match10a64 and match10c64, respectively 49% and 45% faster for the PROSITE
patterns. The improvement for the custom patterns is only 23% and 19%. Furthermore, match10d64 is
also faster than scanning all characters one by one and report all matches for the PROSITE patterns!
match10d64 uses 18.0 seconds while scanning and reporting takes 21.1 seconds. This is not the case for
the 22 custom patterns that have a lot of matches because reporting matches is more expensive in the
parallel versions than the sequential versions because it requires more instructions to get the position of
the match.
17.4.2
Any Maximum Length
Table 17.19 and Table 17.20 show the results for the versions supporting any maximum length.
x86-64 Versions
The results for match01b and match02b are different for the PROSITE patterns and
the custom patterns. For the PROSITE patterns, match01b is 11% faster than match02b. For the custom
patterns, they start relatively even but for all custom patterns match01b is 32% slower than match02b.
match02b is likely faster for the custom patterns because match01b the patterns requires multiple iter-
ations in the inner loop where match01b contains a lot more instructions compared to match02b. The
PROSITE patterns only requires one iteration in the inner loop except for 7 patterns that requires two
iterations. match01b might be faster in those cases because it has the value to check for a match in a
register while match02b needs to store the last word of D and then load it for the check.
Sequential AVX Versions
The results for the sequential AVX versions using multiple vectors are very
similar to results of the single vector counterpart. match03b is faster than match04b because match04b
is slower than expected from the latency chain. match05b is faster than both. For AVX-512, match06b
is the slowest, match07b is faster and match08b is the fastest of the three as expected.
Parallel AVX2 Versions
For the multiple words parallel AVX2 versions, the results changes as the
patterns get longer. For the PROSITE patterns, the match09b08 starts as the fastest for L ≤8 while
match09b64 is clearly the slowest. For the remaining lengths, match09b32 is clearly the fastest while
match09b64 stays slow and match09b08 becomes slowest. For the custom patterns that are much longer,
match09b32 starts as the fastest but match09b64 becomes the fastest for the longest patterns while
match09b08 is the slowest. Hence, for longer patterns, it is more important to have fewer iterations than
high parallelism. Note that match09b16 contains additional operations since there are no msbs in AVX2
for 16-bit elements which might slow it down compared to the others.
Parallel AVX-512 Versions
For the PROSITE patterns, match10b08 is the fastest for the shortest
patterns of the multiple words parallel AVX-512 versions. On the longer PROSITE patterns, match10b32
and match10b64 becomes the fastest. For the custom patterns, match10b64 is the fastest in all cases.
Hence, it seems like 8 simulations in parallel seem like the optimal.

17.4.3
Comparison
Table 17.21 shows the results for a selected subset of versions that includes the naive version, the x86-64
versions, the fastest Specialized Subtraction versions and the fastest parallel AVX versions.
It is seen that it is possible to just use 93% more time (instead of 300%) than match02a to handle 4
times longer patterns using the sequential AVX2 version match05c. With 87% (instead of 700%) more
time than match02a, it is possible to handle 8 times longer patterns using the sequential AVX-512 version
match08c. Thus, match08c can really pay off for longer patterns. Furthermore, it should be noted that
it is for the PROSITE patterns with 64 ≤L meaning that match05c and match08c can also handle all
PROSITE patterns.
On the other hand, the parallel AVX versions can support the same pattern lengths as match02a but
with significant speedups. Compared to match02a, match09c64 is 63% faster (almost 3 times faster),
match10c64 is 68% faster (over 3 times faster) and match10d64 is almost 83% faster (almost 6 times
faster!).
All the multiple words/vectors versions supports any maximum pattern length, so they are easier to
compare. The naive version is clearly the slowest in all cases except the very long patterns where it is
faster than the multiple words versions because its heuristic helps it to stop as soon as a mismatch is
found. Maybe surprisingly, it is not terribly slow and actually faster than some of the sequential AVX
versions since they do not have heuristics. For the PROSITE patterns, the sequential AVX versions
are slower than the multiple words versions, but when the patterns get long enough as for the custom
patterns, they are actually faster. However, the 64-bit parallel AVX versions are the clear winners with
match10b64 being the fastest in all cases. The advantage of the parallel versions is that they only process
64 bits per iteration in the inner loop compared to 512 bits for match08b. This allows to waste fewer
bits to simulate the patterns in the inner loop while performing 8 simulations in parallel to still utilize
the full potential of AVX.
To sum up, the best version to handle all (PROSITE) patterns with 64 ≤L is clearly match10d
because it was even faster than the time for scanning the whole text and reporting all matches. However,
this version exploited the binary representation of the characters in the alphabet to efficiently calculate
the indexes in the lookup table. In case this is not possible, match10c64 can be used, but it is almost
twice as slow. In all other cases, match10b64 is the fastest. It is even more than twice as fast as match01a
that only supports patterns satisfying L ≤64 while match10b64 supports any maximum pattern length.
Hence, the most efficient way to utilize AVX is to parallelize the simulation rather than using AVX to
hold larger automaton states.

18
Future Work
Based on the experimental results for match01b and match02b, it could be interesting to check if match02b
becomes faster than match01b in all cases if the current word of D is kept in a register. However, the
difference in performance may also be due to external factors. In any case, they should be tested again
to determine what that causes match02b to be slower than match01b for the PROSITE patterns.
In this thesis, only the forward algorithm by Gonzalo Navarro and Mathieu Raffinot in 2003 in [5] was
considered. There is also a backward algorithm that also simulates a reverse automaton in a similar way
to the forward algorithm. The reverse automaton works as a heuristic that might enable the algorithm
to skip characters in T. This kind of improvement was also observed for the naive algorithm (match00)
that was considerably faster than its theoretical complexity. However, the backward algorithm has to use
the forward algorithm on the parts of T where there may be a match to check if there actually is a match
which makes it theoretically slower. It would be interesting to see if that could provide an additional
speed up, since the backward algorithm would likely be able to skip many characters when there are very
few matches. Very early in the project, some versions of the backward algorithm were implemented and
tested in some less structured ways using the PROSITE patterns. There were backward versions roughly
corresponding to early counterparts of the versions match01x, match02x, match03x and match05x. Most
tests showed that the backward algorithm was slightly to notably faster than the corresponding forward
variant. However, the best versions were found to be the parallel versions. In the forward algorithm,
the text can easily be split into equal parts that use the same time. The same is not possible in the
backward algorithm since it may skip characters for one part while another part has to check for a match
with the forward algorithm for every character. Hence, the parts may have to wait for each other to
synchronize such that they can run either the forward or backward check in parallel, but then the slowest
part in each iteration determines the speed. A perhaps far better approach to utilize the parallelism in
AVX is to split the text T into X parts, run the backward algorithm in parallel for each part and save
all positions found by the backward algorithm instead of running the forward algorithm right after to
check for a match. After running the backward algorithm in parallel, then all potential matches can be
checked by the forward algorithm to fully utilize the parallelism in AVX. However, there might still be
some parts that are slower than the others, so it might be a good idea to create X large tasks and then
perhaps 2X smaller tasks that can be distributed to the parts that finishes first. It adds more overhead
in the form of rescanned characters and managing assigning of new parts. On the other hand, this might
utilize more of the bandwidth in AVX and the overhead of extra characters is only L −1 per extra task
which is likely very small compared to n. Hence, there are also many aspects in finding the best way to
run the backward algorithm – especially when running it in parallel.
107
==PAGE:109==
19
Conclusion
The algorithm by Navarro and Raffinot for the Character classes and Bounded Gaps Pattern Matching
Problem has been considered. The algorithm has been extended to also allow repeated character classes
and negated repeated character classes to better support the patterns in the PROSITE database. This
was achieved just by modifying the preprocessing without affecting its complexity. The preprocessing
was also greatly improved to only use linear time in the pattern size and size of the alphabet, namely
O(|P| + |Σ|), instead of using O(L|Σ|) time when L ≤w.
The original algorithm also had three unhandled edge cases. The first problem was that the bitmasks
F and I might have conflicting bits which results in incorrect simulation of the ϵ-transitions. This was
fixed by clearing the conflicting bits in F when they where present in D & I. The second edge case was
that successive bounded gaps could also result in incorrect simulation of the ϵ-transitions which is simply
fixed by adding the constraint that all successive bounded gaps are joined into one gap. The last edge
case was to handle bounded gaps in the start and end by eventually skipping the first characters in T
and ignore the out of bounds bit in F.
Navarro and Raffinot also presented how to deal with patterns that has L > w by using multiple
words and keep track of carries. A SIMD model for operating on vectors of X elements of w bits each
was presented. The model was first used to allow patterns with L ≤X ∗w by using single vectors to
represent (X ∗w)-bit integers. The main challenges were to efficiently perform left bit shift by one and
(X ∗w)-bit subtraction. Here, three different ways to perform the subtraction was presented. The fastest
subtraction method that only uses 4 operations was Specialized Subtraction that exploits properties in
the two operands to already in the preprocessing determine which elements that may generate carries and
the elements that should receive the carries. It was also shown how to extend the single vector algorithms
to use multiple vectors by also handling carries between vectors. The SIMD model can also be used to
perform X operations in parallel. This gave rise to a parallel version of the algorithm that splits the text
into X parts with some overlap between the parts to account for matching spanning multiple parts. The
parallel version was further expanded to also allow any pattern length by using multiple words similar to
the version with multiple words by Navarro and Raffinot.
The theoretical original algorithms and the algorithms using the SIMD model were implemented in
practice using the x86-64, AVX2 and AVX-512 instructions sets. Their matching loops were analyzed
in terms of their latency chain that is the total latency of the longest dependency chain to estimate
their performance in practices on a specific CPU. Furthermore, some versions were also optimized in
assembly and one parallel version stored the lookup table in AVX vector register. The latency chains
reflected the relative performance in the experimental tests of the different versions except in those cases
where the latency data was too uncertain. The experimental results showed that the AVX-512 version
using Specialized Subtractions only used 87% more time compared to the original single word version
but supports eight times longer patterns. The parallel AVX-512 version with 8 elements in parallel was
over 3 times faster than the single word version when L ≤w. Storing the lookup table in vector registers
could make it almost twice as fast to gain a 6 times speed up compared to the single word version. In
case any pattern length should be allowed, it is also the parallel AVX-512 version using multiple words
with 8 elements in parallel that is the fastest option and it is even more than twice as fast as the single
word version. Hence, the best option to utilize X parallel w-bit registers is to use them in parallel rather
than trying to use them as one large (X ∗w)-bit register.
